As a Gold Coast student, we want to make sure that you are aware of the laws, rules, and codes that affect your license. While the following are not new law changes, they are nevertheless very important laws and rules you should be aware of to protect your license and avoid fines. ‚Ä¢ As you know, sole proprietors or partnerships cannot be exempt from the workers‚Äô compensation insurance requirement. For corporations, the exemption is still allowed for up to three corporate officers who each own 10% or more of the company‚Äôs stock. Realize that a total of three exemptions is also the limit for any ‚Äúaffiliated‚Äù grouping of companies (companies where the same individuals have ownership). ‚Ä¢ ‚ÄúAdvance Module‚Äù Building Code update courses remain a part of the CE requirement of most boards. Contractors need one hour; architects need two hours. Our program includes two of these ‚ÄúAdvanced Module‚Äù hours. ‚Ä¢ Computer tracking is in place and 100% compliance is in force. The renewal of your license WILL NOT be issued until you have completed all of the required CE hours. The statewide system tracks license history, CE, and sanctions for both certified and registered contractors. It is important for you to establish an online account with the DBPR. You can do this by visiting myfloridalicense.com. Once your account is established, it is equally important to select the ‚ÄúProfile Merge‚Äù feature. This will ensure that informational changes to one license will be carried forward to all of your other licenses, if applicable. FOR DETAILED INFORMATION SCAN QR CODES THROUGHT THE PUBLICATIONvi Reicon Publishing, LLC [This Page Left Blank Intentionally]Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 Chapter 1 WORKPLACE SAFETY 1 2 INTRODUCTION TO OSHA 3 4 OSHA Act of 1970 5 6 The Act: ‚ÄúTo assure safe and healthful working conditions for working men and women; by authorizing 7 enforcement of the standards developed under the Act; by assisting and encouraging the States in their 8 efforts to assure safe and healthful working conditions; by providing for research, information, education, 9 and training in the field of occupational safety and health; and for other purposes. Be it enacted by the 10 Senate and House of Representatives of the United States of America in Congress assembled, that this 11 Act may be cited as the Occupational Safety and Health Act of 1970 (OSH Act). 12 13 OSHA‚Äôs Mission 14 15 With the OSH Act, Congress created the Occupational Safety and Health Administration (OSHA) to 16 assure safe and healthful working conditions for working men and women by setting and enforcing 17 standards and by providing training, outreach, education, and assistance. 18 19 The OSH Act was passed to prevent workers from being killed or seriously harmed at work. This law 20 created the OSHA that sets and enforces protective workplace safety and health standards. OSHA also 21 provides information, training, and assistance to employers and workers. 22 23 Under the OSH Act, employers have the responsibility to provide a safe workplace. 24 25 In the field of training, over 35 years ago, OSHA established the Outreach Training Program with the 26 introduction of OSHA 10 hours and OSHA 30 hours. Over the past several years, more than 1.6 million 27 students have been trained. Today, the outreach program has a national network of over 16,000 28 independent trainers. 29 30 Federal OSHA Coverage and Statistics 31 32 OSHA is an official entity where all worker‚Äôs complaints should be heard and addressed accordingly. 33 34 It is the agency responsible for providing research, information, education, and training guidelines for any 35 area related to occupational safety and health. 36 37 OSHA provides standards for many industries; however, for this course we focus on the standards 38 relative to the construction industry, 29 CFR 1903, 1910, and primarily, 1926. 39 40 5,333 workers died on the job in 2019 https://www.bls.gov/news.release/cfoi.nr0.htm (3.5 per 100,000 full- 41 time equivalent workers) ‚Äî on average, more than 100 a week or about 15 deaths every day. About 20% 42 (1,061) of worker fatalities in private industry in calendar year 2019 were in construction ‚Äì accounting for 43 one in five worker deaths for the year. 44 45 The 5,333 fatal occupational injuries in 2019 represents the largest annual number since 2007. 46 Fatalities in the private construction industry increased 5 percent to 1,061‚Äì the largest total since 2007. Machinery and Machine Guarding, general requirements (29 CFR 1910.212) 13 14 Effectiveness of the OSHA Mandate 15 16 In more than four decades, OSHA and its state partners, coupled with the efforts of employers, safety and 17 health professionals, unions, and advocates, have had a dramatic effect on workplace safety. 18 19 Worker deaths in America are down on average, from about 38 worker deaths a day in 1970 to 15 a day 20 in 2019. Worker injuries and illnesses are down from 10.9 incidents per 100,000 full-time equivalent 21 workers in 1972 to 2.8 per 100,000 in 2019. 22 23 OSHA SAFETY STANDARDS - WHAT‚ÄôS NEW? 24 25 The OSHA standards are contained in Title 29 of the Code of Federal Regulations (CFR) or written as ‚Äú29 26 CFR.‚Äù OSHA provides standards for many industries; however, for this course we focus on the standards 27 relative to the construction industry, 29 CFR 1903, 1910, and primarily, 1926. These are the specific 28 OSHA standards that regulate and affect various types of work environments and activities: 29 30 ‚Ä¢ Part 1926: Construction is regulated by Part 1926 that affects new construction, major renovation work, 31 and demolition work. 32 ‚Ä¢ Part 1903: Part 1903 affects inspections, citations, and proposed penalties. 33 ‚Ä¢ Part 1904: Part 1904 affects recording and reporting occupational injuries and illness. 34 ‚Ä¢ Part 1910: Part 1910 affects general industry standards. 35 36 Each part is further subdivided into subparts that are organized alphabetically. Each clause starts with 37 1926 and is then divided into paragraph, then several sub-sub paragraphs. The typical example is 38 1926.453(a)(1)(i)[A] 39 40 Confined Spaces in Construction 41 42 New sections were added. This standard took effect in 2015. 43 Examples of locations where confined spaces may occur include, but are not limited to, the following: 44 45 ‚Ä¢ Air Preheaters ÔÇ° Air Receivers 46 ‚Ä¢ Boilers ÔÇ° Cesspools 47 ‚Ä¢ Chillers ÔÇ° Concrete pier columns 48 ‚Ä¢ Enclosed Beams ÔÇ° Heating, ventilation, and air 49 ‚Ä¢ Incinerators ÔÇ° conditioning (HVAC) ducts 50 ‚Ä¢ Lift Stations ÔÇ° Mixer Reactors 51 ‚Ä¢ Pits (such as elevator, escalator, pump, equipment) ÔÇ° Water mains 52 ‚Ä¢ Precast concrete and other preformed valve or other manhole units 53 ‚Ä¢ Tanks (such as fuel, chemical, water or other liquid, solid, or gas) 54 ‚Ä¢ VesselsFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 1 - Workplace Safety Reicon Publishing, LLC 3 1 Cranes and Derricks in Construction: 1926.1427 2 3 This is a new section. Implementation date June 23, 2017, Enforcement September 23, 2017. 18 19 EMPLOYER RESPONSIBILITY 20 21 This is a short summary of key employer responsibilities: 22 Please remember that the prime contractor assumes all obligations prescribed as employer. 23 ‚Ä¢ Provide a workplace free from serious recognized hazards and comply with standards, rules and 24 regulations issued under the OSH Act. 25 ‚Ä¢ Examine workplace conditions to make sure they conform to applicable OSHA standards. 26 ‚Ä¢ Make sure employees have and use safe tools and equipment and properly maintain this equipment. 27 ‚Ä¢ Use color codes, posters, labels, or signs to warn employees of potential hazards. 28 ‚Ä¢ Establish or update operating procedures and communicate them so that employees follow safety and 29 health requirements. 30 ‚Ä¢ Employers must provide safety training in a language and vocabulary workers can understand. 31 ‚Ä¢ Employers with hazardous chemicals in the workplace must develop and implement a written hazard 32 communication program and train employees on the hazards they are exposed to and proper precautions 33 (and a copy of safety data sheets must be readily available). See the OSHA page on Hazard 34 Communication. 35 ‚Ä¢ Provide medical examinations and training when required by OSHA standards. 36 ‚Ä¢ Post, at a prominent location within the workplace, the OSHA poster (or the state-plan equivalent) 37 informing employees of their rights and responsibilities. 38 ‚Ä¢ Keep records of work-related injuries and illnesses. (Note: Employers with ten or fewer employees and 39 employers in certain low-hazard industries are exempt from this requirement. 40 ‚Ä¢ Report to the nearest OSHA office all work-related fatalities within eight hours, and all work-related 41 inpatient hospitalizations, all amputations and all losses of an eye within 24 hours. Call the OSHA toll-free 42 number 1-800-321-OSHA (6742); TTY 1-877-889-5627. [Employers under federal OSHA's jurisdiction 43 were required to begin reporting by January 1, 2015. Establishments in a state with a state-run OSHA 44 program should contact their state plan for the implementation date]. 45 ‚Ä¢ Provide employees, former employees and their representatives access to the Log of Work-Related 46 Injuries and Illnesses (OSHA Form 300). On February 1, and for three months, covered employers must 47 post the summary of the OSHA log of injuries and illnesses (OSHA Form 300A). 48 ‚Ä¢ Provide access to employee medical records and exposure records to employees or their authorized 49 representatives. 50 ‚Ä¢ Provide to the OSHA compliance officer the names of authorized employee representatives who may be 51 asked to accompany the compliance officer during an inspection. 52 ‚Ä¢ Not discriminate against employees who exercise their rights under the Act. See the OSHA 53 "Whistleblower Protection" webpage. 54 ‚Ä¢ Post OSHA citations at or near the work area involved. Each citation must remain posted until the 55 violation has been corrected, or for three working days, whichever is longer. Post abatement verification 56 documents or tags. 57 ‚Ä¢ Correct cited violations by the deadline set in the OSHA citation and submit required abatementChapter 1 - Workplace Safety Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 4 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 verification documentation. 2 3 OSHA encourages all employers to adopt an injury and illness prevention program. These programs, 4 known by a variety of names, are universal interventions that can substantially reduce the number and 5 severity of workplace injuries and alleviate the associated financial burdens on U.S. workplaces. Also, 6 numerous employers in the United States already manage safety using injury and illness prevention 7 programs, and we believe that all employers can and should do the same. Most successful programs are 8 based on a common set of key elements. These include management leadership, worker participation, 9 hazard identification, hazard prevention and control, education and training, and program evaluation and 10 improvement. 11 12 SAFETY PROGRAMS 13 14 A written safety program is a very useful and essential tool to follow all OSHA requirements. It can also 15 provide a discount in your workers‚Äô compensation insurance premium. If you don‚Äôt have one, we suggest 16 that you implement one. In Florida, you can take advantage of the easily available toolkit from the 17 University of South Florida found at: 18 https://health.usf.edu/publichealth/cohpe/usfsafetyflorida/our-online-applications/safetywriter 19 20 Also, we suggest that you buy an OSHA book for each of your supervisors as part of their hiring process 21 and make it mandatory. You will surely see the difference. 22 23 Important topics to include in a safety program are: 24 25 ‚Ä¢ Control and prevention hazards ÔÇ° Emergency response plans 26 ‚Ä¢ First and CPR trained personnel ÔÇ° Goals and objectives of the program 27 ‚Ä¢ Identification of potential hazards on the jobsite ÔÇ° Inspections, training, accident reporting and 28 investigation 29 ‚Ä¢ Job hazard assessment ÔÇ° Management commitment 30 ‚Ä¢ Personal protective equipment ÔÇ° Policies and procedures 31 ‚Ä¢ Record keeping ÔÇ° Safety & health training, employee orientation 32 ‚Ä¢ Unit safety responsibilities 33 ‚Ä¢ Vehicle safety and use 34 35 Advantages of a safety program include: 36 37 ‚Ä¢ Insurance Savings ÔÇ° Savings in lost time and productivity due to accidents 38 ‚Ä¢ Good management tool to assure compliance 39 ‚Ä¢ Enhancement of workers‚Äô safety awareness 40 TOP 10 CITED STANDARDS 41 42 These statistics have been compiled from the Department of Labor as of December 2020. 43 44 The following were the top 10 most frequently cited standards by Federal OSHA in fiscal year 2020: 45 1. We 6 suggest that you take the time to watch them. https://www.osha.gov/vtools/construction 7 8 SIGNIFICANT CHANGES IN THE RESIDENTIAL FALL PROTECTION 9 POLICY 10 Fall is the major cause of injuries in construction. It is important that we review the requirements for 11 residential construction where, most of the time, supervision is minimal or missing. 12 13 Under the new directive, employers must follow 1926.501(b)(13). This rule states that, ‚Äú‚Ä¶ Each employee 14 engaged in residential construction activities 6 feet (1.8 m) or more above lower levels shall be protected 15 by guardrail systems, safety net system, or personal fall arrest system ‚Ä¶‚Äù or, by alternative fall protection 16 measures allowed under 1926.501(b) for particular types of work as outlined below: 17 18 ‚Ä¢ 1926.501(b)(2)(ii): Controlled access zones and control lines can be used for some leading-edge 19 applications. 20 ‚Ä¢ 1926.501(b)(4)(i) and (ii): Covers can be used to prevent workers from falling through holes. 21 ‚Ä¢ 1926.501(b)(5): Positioning devices can be used while working on the face of formwork or reinforcing 22 steel. 23 ‚Ä¢ 1926.501(b)(7)(i) and (ii): Barricades, fences and covers can be used to prevent workers from falling into 24 excavations. 25 ‚Ä¢ 1926.501(b)(8)(i): Equipment guards can be used to prevent workers from falling into dangerous 26 equipment. 27 ‚Ä¢ 1926.501(b)(10): A combination of a warning line system and safety monitoring system can be used for 28 roofing work on low-slope (4:12 or less) roofs. Or, on roofs 50-feet (15.25 m) or less in width, the use of a 29 safety monitoring system without a warning line system is permitted. 30 31 If the employer can demonstrate that it is infeasible or creates a greater hazard to use the required fall 32 protection systems, the employer must instead develop and implement a written site-specific fall 33 protection plan in accordance with 1926.502(k). OSHA does not consider "economic infeasibility" to be 34 a basis for failing to provide conventional fall protection. 35 36 Note: There is a presumption that it is feasible and will not create a greater hazard to implement at 37 least one of the fall protection systems listed in 1926.501(b)(13). 38 39 OSHA expects that the fall protection methods listed in 1926.501(b)(13) can be used without 40 significant safety or feasibility problems for the vast majority of residential construction activities. 41 42 OSHA did not define residential construction in 1926.501(b)(13). STD 03-11-002 includes an 43 interpretation for the term ‚Äúresidential construction‚Äù for purposes of the standard. 44Chapter 1 - Workplace Safety Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 6 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 Residential Construction Definition 2 3 In order to be classified as residential construction, two of the following elements must be met: 4 ‚Ä¢ The end-use of the structure being built must be as a home, i.e., a dwelling. 5 ‚Ä¢ The structure being built must be constructed using traditional wood frame construction materials and 6 methods. 7 ‚Ä¢ The limited use of steel I-beams to help support wood framing does not disqualify a structure from being 8 considered residential construction. 9 ‚Ä¢ The use of masonry brick or block in exterior walls are treated as falling within the scope of traditional 10 wood frame construction materials and methods. 11 ‚Ä¢ Cold-formed metal studs will be considered within the bounds of traditional wood frame construction 12 materials and methods. 13 14 Residential Training Requirements 15 16 Under 1926.503, workers exposed to fall hazards must be trained to recognize potential fall hazards 17 and, in the procedures to be followed, to minimize those hazards. 18 19 Training must cover the following among other subjects: 20 21 ‚Ä¢ The nature of fall hazards in the work area 22 ‚Ä¢ How to erect, maintain, disassemble, and inspect the fall protection systems to be used 23 ‚Ä¢ How to use and operate the fall protection systems to be used 24 ‚Ä¢ Subpart M requirements 25 ‚Ä¢ Supplements 1926.21 26 ‚Ä¢ Prepare training certification. 27 ‚Ä¢ Documentation of the latest training certification must be maintained. 28 29 Retraining for the following: 30 31 ‚Ä¢ Changes in the fall protection systems to be used or to the workplace 32 ‚Ä¢ Inadequacies in employee‚Äôs knowledge or use of fall protection indicates that retraining is necessary 33 34 RESIDENTIAL CONVENTIONAL FALL PROTECTION SYSTEMS 35 36 The following residential fall systems are outlined below. 37 38 ‚Ä¢ Guardrail systems 39 ‚Ä¢ Safety net systems 40 ‚Ä¢ Personal fall arrest systems 41 42 Residential Guardrail Systems 43 44 Brackets for engineered guardrail systems can either be side mounted or deck mounted. Either way, 45 employers should look to the manufacturer instructions or the recommendations of a registered 46 professional engineer for proper installation. 47 48 Residential Safety Net Systems 49 50 These nets have been positioned to prevent falls to the interior of the building. Employers should consult 51 the manufacturer‚Äôs instructions and/or a registered professional engineer to ensure proper installation of 52 the net and bracing of the stud walls. Give due consideration to the potential impact load on the net and 53 lateral load on the stud walls in the event of a fall. [1926.402(c)].Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 1 - Workplace Safety Reicon Publishing, LLC 7 1 Residential Personal Fall Arrest Systems 2 3 ‚Ä¢ Anchor point: Anchorages used for attachment of Personal Fall Arrest equipment must be 4 capable of supporting at least 5,000 pounds per employee attached, or must be designed and 5 used as follows: 6 o As part of a complete personal fall arrest system which maintains a safety factor of at least 7 two. 8 o Under the supervision of a qualified person. 9 o Employer should look to the manufacturer‚Äôs instructions or the recommendations of a 10 registered professional engineer for proper installation. [1926.502(d)15)] 11 12 ‚Ä¢ Residential PFAS full body harness: A full body harness distributes the force of the fall over the 13 thighs, pelvis, waist, chest, and shoulders. 14 o Body belts have not been allowed as part of an arrest system since January 1998. 15 o Residential PFAS Full Body Harness-Connector/Lanyard 16 o Snap hooks must be locking type. [1926.502(d)(5)] 17 o Never hook two snap hooks together unless designed for that purpose. [1926.502(d)(6)] 18 19 ‚Ä¢ Residential personal fall restraint system: Although the standard does not mention personal 20 fall restraint systems, OSHA has previously stated that it accepts a properly utilized fall restraint 21 system in lieu of a personal fall arrest system when the restraint system is rigged in such a way 22 that prevents the worker from being exposed to the fall hazard. 23 o Fall restraint can be a body belt or full body harness. 24 o Must be tied off so that the worker cannot go past the unprotected side or edge, no matter 25 where the work is on the walking/working surface. 26 o Lanyards should be adjustable to take up slack when workers move about. 27 28 ‚Ä¢ Residential fall protection program - Other methods: 29 o Employers also have the option of having workers work from scaffolds (in compliance with 30 Subpart L), ladders (in compliance with Subpart X) or aerial lifts (in compliance with 31 1926.453) instead of complying with 1926.501(b)(13). 32 o Now we all know the first principle of mishap prevention is to ‚Äúeliminate‚Äù the hazard whenever 33 possible. If you can‚Äôt eliminate it, you then have to minimize its effect. We cannot eliminate 34 working at heights in construction, but using ladders, scaffolds and aerial lifts can help 35 employers eliminate some falls from six feet or higher. 36 o Working at heights cannot be eliminated, but there are ways to minimize exposure to falls. 37 Assemble as many parts of the building as possible on the ground. 38 ‚ñ™ Properly assembled and braced IAW the manufacturer‚Äôs instruction and the 39 BCSI guide 40 ‚ñ™ Flying pre-assembled structures into place will minimize worker exposure to fall 41 hazards 42 ‚ñ™ Do as many things on the ground as possible 43 ‚ñ™ Some contractors are even pre-positioning portions of the guardrail system on 44 the ground 45 ‚ñ™ Pre-installed fall protection 46 47 Residential Fall Hazards - Prevention 48 49 Fall Protection Plan 50 51 If an employer can demonstrate that conventional fall protection is infeasible or presents a greater 52 hazard, the employer shall develop and implement a fall protection plan that complies with 1926.502(k). 53 The employer bears the burden of establishing that it is appropriate to implement a fall protection plan for 54 a particular workplace situation. [1926.502(k)] 55 56 The fall protection plan must meet all of the following requirements. 57 ‚Ä¢ The plan must be written. 58 ‚Ä¢ The plan must be site-specific.Chapter 1 - Workplace Safety Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 8 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 ‚Ä¢ A written fall protection plan developed for repetitive use, e.g., for a particular style or model of home, will 2 be considered site-specific with respect to a particular site only if it fully addresses all issues related to fall 3 protection at that site. Therefore, a standardized plan will have to be reviewed and revised as necessary, 4 on a site-by-site basis. 5 ‚Ä¢ The plan must be prepared by a ‚Äúqualified person.‚Äù [1926.502(k)(1)] 6 ‚Ä¢ It must be developed specifically for the site where the residential construction work is being performed. 7 [1926.502(k)(1)] 8 ‚Ä¢ It must be maintained up to date. [1926.502(k)(1)] 9 ‚Ä¢ All changes must be approved by a qualified person. [1926.502(k)(2)] 10 ‚Ä¢ It must be maintained on the site. [1926.502(k)(3)] 11 ‚Ä¢ It must be implemented under the supervision of a competent person. [1926.502(k)(4)] 12 ‚Ä¢ The plan must include documentation of reasons why conventional fall protection systems are infeasible 13 or create a greater hazard. [1926.502(k)(5)] 14 ‚Ä¢ The plan must include a written discussion of the alternative work practices to be used that will eliminate 15 or reduce the possibility of a fall. [1926.502(k)(6)] 16 ‚Ä¢ It must identify each location where conventional fall protection systems cannot be used and designate 17 them as controlled access zones (CAZ). [1926.502(k)(7)] 18 ‚Ä¢ The plan must implement a safety monitoring system in conformance with 1926.502(h) where no other 19 alternative measure has been implemented. [1926.502(k)(8)] 20 ‚Ä¢ It must identify all workers designated to work in the CAZ. [1926.502(k)(9)] 21 ‚Ä¢ It must be reviewed and updated as appropriate if a fall, or near miss, occurs. [1926.502(k)(10)] 22 23 A sample plan can be found in Part 1926 Subpart M Appendix E on the OSHA Web site at: 24 https://www.osha.gov/laws-regs/regulations/standardnumber/1926/1926SubpartMAppE 25 26 27 CRYSTALLINE SILICA CONSTRUCTION 28 29 In 1938, the Department of Labor produced a film titled "Stop Silicosis." Secretary Frances Perkins 30 concluded that silica dust was dangerous, but it can be prevented. 31 32 Silicosis is a form of occupational lung disease caused by inhalation of crystalline silica dust. It is marked 33 by inflammation and scarring in the form of nodular lesions in the upper lobes of the lungs. It is a type of 34 pneumoconiosis. 35 36 The total deaths in the US due to Silicosis during the period between 2005 through 2014 was of 1,167 37 deaths, mortality rate is down from a high of 161 in 2005 to 84 deaths in 2014. 38 39 Those that were exposed didn't know then, and many still don't know now. For additional information, 40 view this video: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HAByIIzQSuU 41 42 43 About 1.85 million workers are currently exposed to respirable crystalline silica in construction 44 workplaces. Over 640,000 of these workers are estimated to be exposed to silica levels that exceed 45 OSHA's proposed permissible exposure limit (PEL). 46 47 These exposures occur during common construction operations, such as: 48 ‚Ä¢ Drywall finishing using silica containingFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 1 - Workplace Safety Reicon Publishing, LLC 9 1 ‚Ä¢ Operating vehicle-mounted drilling rigs material 2 ‚Ä¢ Rock Crushing 3 ‚Ä¢ Tuck pointing 4 ‚Ä¢ Using hand operated grinders 5 ‚Ä¢ Using Heavy equipment during earthmoving 6 ‚Ä¢ Using masonry saws 7 ‚Ä¢ Using jackhammers 8 ‚Ä¢ Using rotary hammers or drills 9 10 A proposed rule for new silica construction provisions is expected to save nearly 700 lives and prevent 11 1,600 new cases of silicosis per year once the full effects of the rule are realized. 12 13 Major Provisions of the New Silica Construction Standard 14 15 The new standard for silica construction that went into effect on September 23, 2017, includes provisions 16 for employers to do the following: 17 ‚Ä¢ Measure the amount of silica that workers are exposed to if it may be at or above an action level of 25 18 Œºg/m3 (micrograms of silica per cubic meter of air), averaged over an eight-hour day 19 ‚Ä¢ Protect workers from respirable crystalline silica exposures above the PEL of 50 Œºg/m3, averaged over an 20 eight-hour day 21 ‚Ä¢ Limit workers' access to areas where they could be exposed above the PEL 22 ‚Ä¢ Use dust controls to protect workers from silica exposures above the PEL 23 ‚Ä¢ Provide respirators to workers when dust controls cannot limit exposures to the PEL 24 ‚Ä¢ Offer medical exams, including chest X-rays and lung function tests every three years for workers 25 exposed above the PEL for 30 or more days per year 26 ‚Ä¢ Train workers on work operations that result in silica exposure and ways to limit exposure 27 ‚Ä¢ Keep records of workers' silica exposure and medical exams [1926.1153] 28 29 How to Control the Exposure? 30 31 Employers must use engineering controls and work practices as the primary way to keep exposures at or 32 below the PEL. Engineering controls include wetting down work operations or using local exhaust 33 ventilation (such as vacuums) to keep silica-containing dust out of the air and out of workers' lungs. 34 Another control method that may work well is enclosing an operation or "process isolation.‚Äù Respirators 35 are only allowed when engineering and work practice controls cannot maintain exposures at or below the 36 PEL. 37 38 For construction, the standard includes Table 1, a list of common construction tasks along with exposure 39 control methods and work practices that work well for those tasks and can be used to comply with the 40 requirements of the standard. 41 42 Please review the Table at the end of this section for the different ways in which to control exposure. 43 44 Regardless of which exposure control method is used, all construction employers covered by the 45 standard are required to do the following: 46 47 Establish and implement a written exposure control plan that identifies tasks that involve exposure and 48 methods used to protect workers, including procedures to restrict access to work areas where high 49 exposures may occur 50 51 Designate a competent person to implement the written exposure control plan 52 Restrict housekeeping practices that expose workers to silica where feasible alternatives are available 53 Offer medical exams, including chest X-rays and lung function tests every three years for workers who 54 are required by the standard to wear a respirator for 30 or more days per year 55 Train workers on work operations that result in silica exposure and ways to limit exposure 56 Keep records of workers‚Äô silica exposure and medical exams 57 58 OSHA‚Äôs Crystalline Silica Rule (Construction)Chapter 1 - Workplace Safety Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 10 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 2 Revised in 2020 ‚Äì Provides a summary of the requirements of the respirable crystalline silica standard 3 for construction. 4 5 OSHA Directives: Inspection Procedures for the Respirable 6 Crystalline Silica Standards 7 8 New effective date: 06/25/2020 ‚Äì The purpose of this Instruction is to provide guidelines and establish 9 uniform inspection and enforcement procedures for the respirable crystalline silica standards. The two 10 silica standards, 29 CFR 1910.1053 (General Industry and Maritime) and 29 DFR 1926.1153 11 (Construction), published in the Federal Register/ Vol. 81 No. 58/ Friday March 25, 2016, became 12 effective on June 23, 2016. 13 14 Significant Changes. This Instruction outlines the changes in enforcement due to the publication of the 15 silica standards in the Federal Register, 81 FR at 16285 (Mar. 25, 2016). The silica standards establish a 16 PEL of 50 ¬µg/m¬≥ and an action level (AL) of 25 ¬µg/m¬≥ for the three major forms of crystalline silica (i.e., 17 quartz, cristobalite, and tridymite1 ) for the general, maritime, and construction industries. 18 19 The new PEL represents a change from the preceding PELs, which were based on formulas and were 20 inconsistent between industries and forms of silica. This change is particularly significant with regard to 21 the preceding PEL for the construction and shipyard industries, which was based on a particle count 22 method, expressed in terms of millions of particles per cubic foot (mppcf), and required the use of a 23 conversion factor to yield results reported in mg/m¬≥. 24 25 OSHA INSPECTIONS AND PENALTIES SUMMARY 26 27 Site Specific Targeting Lists 28 29 ‚Ä¢ National emphasis programs ÔÇ° Combustible dust (sawdust) 30 ‚Ä¢ Trenching ÔÇ° Hexavalent chromium (welding fumes) 31 ‚Ä¢ Workplace violence ÔÇ° Process safety management (PSM) 32 ‚Ä¢ Silica (concrete sawing) ÔÇ° Primary metals (lead) 33 ‚Ä¢ Amputations ÔÇ° Isocyanates (industrial solvents) 34 35 Inspection Priorities 36 37 ‚Ä¢ Imminent danger ÔÇ° Catastrophes and fatal accidents 38 ‚Ä¢ Employee complaint ÔÇ° Referrals 39 ‚Ä¢ Programmed high hazard inspections ÔÇ° Follow up inspections 40 41 Penalties 42 43 These are as of January 15, 2021. 44 Type of Violation Maximum Penalty Amount Serious $13,653 Other $13,653 Willful/Repeat $136,532 Posting Requirements $13,653 Failure to Abate/Day $13,653 45 RECORDKEEPING 46 47 OSHA section 1904: Recording and reporting occupational injuries and illnesses 48 ‚Ä¢ Form 300: Log of Work-Related Injuries and Illnesses 49 ‚Ä¢ Form 300A: Summary of Work-Related Injuries and Illnesses 50Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 1 - History 7 ‚Ä¢ Legislative Changes to F.S. 440 8 ‚Ä¢ Compliance Requirements 9 ‚Ä¢ Coverage Options 10 ‚Ä¢ Exemptions: 11 ‚Ä¢ Advantages/Disadvantages 12 ‚Ä¢ Assistance from Division of Workers‚Äô Compensation (DWC) 13 ‚Ä¢ Employers FAQs 14 ‚Ä¢ Prohibited Activities 15 16 OVERVIEW HISTORY 17 18 USA 19 20 10th Century B.C. - Kings and Temples and Book of Genesis, first possible indication of a form of 21 workman's compensation 22 23 1855 - Georgia, passed the Employer Liability Act in the state legislature. 24 25 1855-1907 - 26 other states passed similar acts that simply gave permission to sue the employer if the 26 employee proved a negligent act or omission. 27 28 1897 - England repealed the Employer's Liability Act and replaced it with a Working Man‚Äôs Compensation 29 Act. 30 31 1898 - New York, the New York Social Club drafted a bill for the Partial Compensation for Workers. No 32 action was taken by the state legislature. The largest opponent was the labor unions. 33 34 1901 - Maryland passed legislation for a Cooperative Accident Insurance Fund. 35 36 1905 - The Maryland Act was ruled as unconstitutional by the state Supreme Court. 37 38 1908 - The Federal Employer's Liability Act was passed by the U.S. Congress at the urging of President 39 Theodore Roosevelt. This was the first workman‚Äôs compensation law in the U.S. 40 41 1911 - New York, Triangle Shirtwaist Company fire happened. Over 146 workers jumped to their deaths 42 to escape the fire in the ten-story building because the exits were blocked. This resulted in many lawsuits. 43 The entire nation was shocked by the tragedy. New York City immediately adopted the first safety codes. 44 45 1911 - Wisconsin became the first state to adopt a true workman's compensation law. It was called the 46 Great Trade Off. Employers provided coverage; employees gave up the right to sue. 47 48 1912 - Four more states passed laws. 49 50 1913 - Eight more states adopted legislation. 51 52 1915 - Alaska and Hawaii passed workman‚Äôs compensation laws even though they were only territories.Chapter 2 - Workers‚Äô Compensation Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 14 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 2 1935 - Florida passed the Workman's Compensation Law. 3 4 Florida 5 6 1935 - Florida‚Äôs Workman‚Äôs Compensation Law became effective. 7 8 1978 -1979 - Major changes in the state workers‚Äô compensation system were underway in the state 9 legislature, the first major change since 1935. The fixed benefit system was replaced, and the Wage Loss 10 Concept was introduced. Workman's compensation was changed to workers' compensation. 11 12 1990 - The Division of Fraud and the first Drug Free Workplace were established. 13 14 Later - The Bureau of Safety was introduced. Managed health care, employee assistance office, and 15 emphasis on reemployment was introduced. 16 17 1993 - Wage loss was eliminated. Supplemental benefits, managed care, chiropractic care limits, and the 18 ombudsman office were created. 19 20 1999 - The special disability trust fund and division of safety were abolished. 21 22 2003 - The Department of Insurance and Banking merged to become the Department of Financial 23 Services. 24 25 The closing years of the 20th Century brought many changes especially as litigation and medical care 26 continued to be a problem not only in Florida, but throughout the nation as well. 27 28 Since that time, the law has undergone some drastic changes to adapt to all of the other changes that 29 have taken place in our society reflected by the numerous laws enacted by both the US and state 30 legislatures. 31 32 Legislative Intent 33 34 To assure the quick and efficient delivery of disability and medical benefits to an injured worker, an 35 efficient and self-executing system must be created that is not an economic or administrative burden. 36 37 To facilitate the worker‚Äôs return to gainful reemployment at a reasonable cost to the employer. 38 39 The Florida workers‚Äô compensation system is based on a mutual renunciation of common-law rights and 40 defenses by employers and employees alike. 41 42 Workers‚Äô compensation is a no-fault system meant to protect workers and employers. The idea of 43 workers‚Äô compensation was to prevent employees from instituting private lawsuits against employers 44 based on common law. In exchange for providing those benefits, employers generally cannot be sued in 45 the court for causing injuries. 46 47 The Future ‚Ä¶ 48 We are just a few years into the 21st Century and have already seen sweeping changes with the 49 abolishment of the Department of Labor and Employment Security, the Division of Safety, and the Special 50 Disability Trust Fund. We saw many departments merging, created, and so on. 51 We saw the attorney-fee award structure, compliance, exemption, etc. introduced and modified to meet 52 the current needs. 53 Yes, there will be changes as we progress, but workers' compensation is still here for the citizens of 54 Florida.Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 2 - Workers‚Äô Compensation Reicon Publishing, LLC 15 1 Information Sources 2 3 The Florida Statutes: Title XXXI Chapter 440 Workers‚Äô Compensation Law 4 Website: http://leg.state.fl.us/statutes/index.cfm?App_mode=Display_ 5 Statute&URL=0400-0499/0440/0440ContentsIndex.html&StatuteYear=2021& 6 Title=%2D%3E2021%2D%3EChapter%20440 789 10 Workers‚Äô Compensation Insurance 11 Hotline: 1-800-342-1741 12 Website: https://myfloridacfo.com/division/wc/ 13 14 15 16 17 18 Florida Workers‚Äô Compensation Joint Underwriting Association (FWCJUA) 19 Phone: 941-378-7400 20 Website: https://www.fwcjua.com/ 21 22 23 24 25 26 Division of Workers‚Äô Compensation Employee Assistance 27 Bureau of Employee Assistance 28 Phone: 1-800-342-1741 29 Website: https://www.myfloridacfo.com/division/wc/employee/eao.htm 30 31 32 33 34 Fraud reporting sources 35 Division of Insurance Fraud 36 Phone: 1-800-378-0445 (inside Florida) 37 Phone: 1-850-413-3261 (outside Florida) 38 Website: https://myfloridacfo.com/fraudfreeflorida/default.htm 39 40 41 42 43 To File a Non-Compliance report: 44 https://apps.fldfs.com/NonCompliance_Referral/mainpage.aspx 45 46 47 48 49 50 Useful Database : https://dwcdataportal.fldfs.com/POCData.aspx 51 52Chapter 2 - Workers‚Äô Compensation Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 16 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 LEGISLATIVE CHANGES TO F.S. 440 2 3 Changes in 2014 4 5 In 2014, changes were made to House Bill (HB) 271, F.S. 440.107, .15, .16, .49. 6 7 Businesses had five days to respond to the state‚Äôs request for records. That was changed to ten days. 8 9 Information related to an employer's stop-work order should be made available on the Division's website, 10 be updated daily, and remain on the website for at least five years. 11 12 The amount of time investigators could review a company‚Äôs records for not having enough workers‚Äô 13 compensation insurance was changed from three to two years. 14 15 The multiplier for penalties levied was increased from 1.5 to 2. 16 17 Wherever the percentage of ‚Äú66 2/3rd %‚Äù was changed to ‚Äú66.67%‚Äù 18 19 The requirements and deposits were revised in that the Department must calculate the assessment rate 20 for the Special Disability Trust Fund by July 1 of each year. 21 22 Changes now allow companies to make a down payment of just $1,000 on their fine, have payment 23 arrangements for the rest, and arrange for insurance coverage. This was to allow businesses to continue 24 to function while aligning themselves with the law. First time offenders may receive a credit for the initial 25 payment of the estimated annual workers' compensation policy premium, to be applied to the penalty. 26 The $1,000 penalty will be assessed against the employer even if the calculated penalty after the credit 27 has been applied is less than $1,000. This is true even if the coverage is through PEO. Failure by the 28 employer to pay the penalty in full or enter into a payment agreement with the Department within 28 days 29 after service of the stop-work order will result in immediate reinstatement of the stop-work order. 30 31 Changes in 2016 32 33 April 2016 - The Florida Supreme Court overturned a law that had previously limited attorney‚Äôs fees in 34 workers‚Äô compensation court cases. It took the case regarding workers‚Äô compensation attorney‚Äôs fees 35 when a Miami man won an $822.70 compensation after being injured while working for a door making 36 company. The man‚Äôs lawyer, who spent 107 hours on the case, sought a fee of nearly $37,000, but was 37 only awarded $164. The dramatic difference occurred because at the time Florida only allowed attorneys 38 representing injured workers to be paid 20% of the first $5,000 in benefits received, and 15% of the next 39 $5,000. 40 41 April 2016 - The Florida Office of Insurance Regulation (OIR) approved a statewide workers‚Äô 42 compensation insurance rate increase of 14.5% for businesses. The increase was to take effect on 43 December 1, 2016, to affect 2017. 44 45 Currently, temporary disability benefits limit the length of time disability payments can be received for a 46 single injury to two years (104 weeks). In a recent case, the Florida Supreme Court ruled that this two- 47 year cap is unconstitutional and increased it to a five-year cap (270 weeks). 48 49 Changes in 2017 50 51 New section F.S. 440.1851 Personal identifying information of an injured or deceased employee; public 52 records exemption 53 Effective: July 1, 2017 54 55 Synopsis: Personal identifying information of an injured or deceased employee, contained in records by 56 the Department of Financial Services, is confidential and exempt. Disclosure is authorized under specific 57 circumstances 58 Because of the extensive language in this change, you should access a document titled ‚Äú2017 Workers‚Äô 59 Comp Legislative Changes to F.S. 440‚Äù on the DFS / Workers‚Äô Comp website.Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 2 - Workers‚Äô Compensation Reicon Publishing, LLC 17 1 Maximum workers' compensation rate, effective January 1, 2018. $917.18 for the four calendar quarters 2 ending June 30, 2017. [F.S. 440.05] 50 51 Sole proprietors and individuals cannot be exempted. 52 53 The exempted individual may not receive workers‚Äô compensation benefits when they sustain a work- 54 related injury. 55 56 The applicant must be listed as an officer of the corporation in the records of the Florida Department ofFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 2 - Workers‚Äô Compensation Reicon Publishing, LLC 21 1 State, Division of Corporations. [F.S. 440.02(15)(b)(2)] 14 15 A construction industry certificate of exemption issued by the Division is valid for two years after the 16 effective date stated thereon. 17 18 Exemptions are effective on the date they are issued by the Division. 19 20 Obtaining the Exemption 21 22 Obtaining the workers‚Äô compensation exemption is an online process. 23 24 Access the Division of Workers‚Äô Compensation‚Äôs Website: 25 myfloridacfo.com/division/wc/Employer/Exemptions/default.htm 26 Contact customer service at 1-800-742-2214 for any further information. 27 28 An exemption is subject to revocation if the person named on the certificate does not meet eligibility 29 requirements for an exemption. One can voluntarily revoke an exemption by filling out Form DWC 250-R 30 Form DWC 250-R Notice of Revocation to be Exempt 31 32 Exemption Advantages/Disadvantages 33 34 It is important to know the advantages and disadvantages of a workers‚Äô compensation exemption, 35 especially when you see the following statistics. There has been a rise, which is a good indication of 36 construction growth. The rise in non-construction exemptions is due to the statutory change that defines 37 non-construction limited liability company members as ‚Äúcorporate officers,‚Äù that allows them to get the 38 exemption. 39 40 We all know the biggest advantage of the exemption is the monetary benefit. It is important to know the 41 disadvantages of the exemption. 42 43 So, only the disadvantages are listed here: 44 ‚Ä¢ If you are injured, you are on your own. 45 ‚Ä¢ If you hired a sub-contractor with the exemption and they hired a day laborer on a cash-payment basis 46 who gets hurt, you are responsible. 47 ‚Ä¢ If anyone driving a company vehicle has an accident, the vehicle is covered under workers‚Äô 48 compensation, not the company‚Äôs automobile insurance. 49 ‚Ä¢ Think of a scenario where your sub-contractor submits the proof of insurance for insurance that is through 50 the PEO, but the worker who is injured was not reported to the PEO. 51 52 ASSISTANCE FROM DIVISION OF WORKERS‚Äô COMPENSATION (DWC) 53 54 Compliance and Coverage Assistance 55 56 Division of Workers' Compensation Portal provides you with the ability to look up 57 Proof of Coverage information, submit referrals for non-compliance, and toChapter 2 - Workers‚Äô Compensation Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 22 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 interact with Construction Policy Tracking database. All of the following data can 2 be tracked from the following website. 3 4 Website: https://dwcdataportal.fldfs.com/POCData.aspx 5 6 7 8 9 10 WC Construction Policy Tracking Database 11 12 The construction policy-tracking database provides information to contractors regarding the coverage 13 status of the contactors they use. If you have a dispute regarding the risk 36 classification codes, contact the National Council on Compensation Insurance (NCCI) at (800) 622- 37 4123 38 39 Q: Does the injured worker pay any part of my workers' compensation insurance premium? 40 A: The law is very specific on this point. It is the employer‚Äôs responsibility to pay the entire premium for 41 workers' compensation insurance coverage. 42 43 Q: What kinds of employee injuries are covered? 44 A: The law covers all accidental injuries and occupational diseases arising out of and in the course and 45 scope of employment. This includes diseases or infections resulting from such injuries. The law also 46 covers death resulting from such injuries within specified periods of time. Even if you do not think an 47 injury is covered, you must still file the First Report of Injury or Illness (DWC-1) with your insurance 48 carrier within seven days of your first knowledge of the accident/injury for determination of 49 responsibility. 50 51 Q: What injuries are not covered? 52 A: The law does not provide compensation for the following conditions: 53 ‚Ä¢ A mental or nervous injury due to stress, fright, or excitement. 54 ‚Ä¢ A work-related condition that causes an employee to be afraid or dislike for another individual because of 55 the individual's race, color, religion, sex, national origin, age, or handicap. 56 ‚Ä¢ "Pain and suffering" have never been compensable in Florida, nor is it compensable in any other state. 57 ‚Ä¢ The employer may not sue an injured worker for causing a catastrophe nor can the injured worker sue the 58 employer for their injury. This trade-off makes it possible for injured workers to receive immediate medicalFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 2 - Workers‚Äô Compensation Reicon Publishing, LLC 23 1 care, at no cost to the injured worker, without any consideration for who was at fault, the employer or the 2 employee. In civil law, negligence must be established through litigation before any compensation is 3 awarded. [F.S. 440.02(1)] 4 5 Q: Can an employer be liable for double compensation? 6 A: An employer can be liable for double compensation if a minor child is injured while employed in 7 violation of any of the conditions of the child labor laws of Florida. The employer alone, not the 8 insurance carrier, is liable for up to double the normal compensation as provided by the workers' 9 compensation law. To receive further information regarding the Child Labor Law, call the Child Labor 10 Office at (800) 226-2536. 11 12 Q: As a small business owner, can I be sued by an injured worker if I provide all the necessary care, light 13 duty work, and offer to retrain the employee? 14 A: Under the provisions of F.S. 440, an injured worker has two years from the date of the accident to file 15 a petition for benefits with the Division of Administrative Hearings. If an employer provides benefits and 16 return to work options, that should be sufficient to meet the ultimate goal of returning an injured worker 17 to gainful employment. 18 19 However, an employer/carrier's definition of "necessary care" and that of an injured worker may differ. 20 When that happens, the injured worker has no remedy except to file a petition for benefits and have a 21 judge of compensation claims determine whether the benefits that are being provided are sufficient, or if 22 additional benefits not being provided are required by Florida law. If the employer is providing benefits, all 23 expenditures must be reported to the employer's workers' compensation insurance carrier for statistical 24 purposes. 25 26 Q: What in the system would prevent an injured worker, who wanted to leave their employer anyway, from 27 claiming to be hurt, waiting out the treatment, still claiming to be hurt and then trying to settle? 28 A: By law, pain or other subjective complaints alone, in the absence of objective relevant medical 29 findings, are not compensable. However, sometimes these types of claims do occur, and they are 30 sometimes settled by insurance carriers for a nominal amount of money to rid the employer/carrier of a 31 nuisance case. 32 33 Q: Is compensation payable if an employee refuses to use a safety appliance like a hard hat, safety 34 goggles or observe a safety rule? 35 A: Compensation will still be paid, but indemnity benefits (partial wage replacement) may be reduced by 36 25% if the employee knew about the safety rule prior to the accident and failed to observe the rule, or 37 if the employee knowingly chooses not to use a safety appliance which the employer has directed him 38 to use. [F.S. 440.09(5))] 39 40 Q: Will becoming a drug-free workplace save me money on my workers‚Äô compensation insurance 41 premium? 42 A: If you implement a drug-free workplace program in accordance with the criteria set forth in F.S. 43 440.102, you may be eligible for a 5% premium credit on your workers‚Äô compensation insurance 44 premium. In addition to the premium credit, having a workers' compensation drug-free workplace 45 program may make your workplace safer, resulting in fewer accidents, which may reduce your 46 workers‚Äô compensation costs. 47 48 Q: Under the workers‚Äô compensation drug-free workplace program, can I conduct random drug testing of 49 my employees? 50 A: In addition to the situations in which testing is mandatory, the law does not prohibit a private employer 51 from conducting random testing or any other lawful testing of employees. A public employer may 52 institute random testing of employees in "safety sensitive" or "special risk" occupations. 53 54 PROHIBITED ACTIVITIES 55 56 F.S. 440.105(2) Prohibited Activities; Reports; Penalties; Limitations 57 58 Whoever violates any provision of this subsection commits a misdemeanor of the first degree, punishable 59 as provided in F.S 775.082 or F.S. 775.083. 60Chapter 2 - Financial mismanagement or misconduct occurs when: 43 44 Valid liens have been recorded against the property of a contractor‚Äôs customer for supplies or services 45 ordered by the contract for the customer‚Äôs job; the contractor has received funds from the customer to 46 pay for the supplies or services; and the contractor has not had the liens removed from the property, by 47 payment or by bond, within 75 days after the date of such liens; 48 49 The contractor has abandoned a customer‚Äôs job and the percentage of completion is less than the 50 percentage of the total contract price paid to the contractor as of the time of abandonment, unless the 51 contractor is entitled to retain such funds under the terms of the contract or refunds the excess funds 52 within 30 days after the date the job is abandoned; or 53 54 The contractor‚Äôs job has been completed, and it is shown that the customer has had to pay more for the 55 contracted job than the original contract price, as adjusted for subsequent change orders, unless such 56 increase in cost was the result of circumstances beyond the control of the contractor, was the result of 57 circumstances caused by the customer, or was otherwise permitted by the terms of the contract between 58 the contractor and the customer. 59 60 Monies Received by Contractors [F.S. 489.126]Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 3 - Find the right people with the right skills and 31 personalities to be part of your team. 32 33 You are not the Energizer Bunny! Make sure you have a plan for recharging your batteries and keeping 34 up the motivation, you will need to make your dream business happen. 35 36 When you become self-employed, you become responsible for designing, producing, selling, customer 37 care, financing, collecting bad debts, bookkeeping, etc. But it is not essential that you undertake all of 38 these tasks unaided. It may be more cost-effective to allow professional and experienced people in 39 particular sectors to assume some ‚Äòchores‚Äô with which you are not comfortable and thus allow you the 40 time to undertake those with which you are, paying for their work from the increased turnover you are now 41 capable of earning. 42 43 Good practices to develop a good team of workers for your company include: 44 ‚Ä¢ Provide on-going education and training for workers 45 ‚Ä¢ Cross-train workers to build bench strength 46 ‚Ä¢ Communicate the direction and goals daily 47 ‚Ä¢ Create a work culture that enjoys and laughs 48 ‚Ä¢ Institute a meaningful incentive planChapter 3 - Successful Business Practices Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 34 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 Provide On-Going Education and Training for Workers 2 3 ‚ÄúOn-going‚Äù suggests a continuous effort to train whenever they can. 4 5 For the seasonal contractor, this might mean bringing their workers in a few days to a few weeks earlier 6 than opening day and taking them through a series of training topics. 7 8 Every contractor should be ready for those rainy days when grabbing an extra few hours on some 9 education would benefit the crews. 10 11 You are taking this course not only to comply with the continuing education requirements of your license, 12 but also to learn about new changes in the statutes, building codes, etc. affecting your license. Your 13 workers deserve the same consideration. On-going training and education on safety, new methods of 14 doing things, seminars conducted by speakers who will discuss these topics, will be most beneficial not 15 only to your employees but also to your company. 16 17 Cross-Train to Build Bench Strength 18 19 The greater diversity that your workers can handle, the easier time of scheduling you will experience. 20 Having more than one worker who can competently handle a paver, sealcoat machine, roller, or backhoe, 21 for example, the better chance your productivity will stay strong and profitable. This certainly is one of 22 signs of a successful construction contractor. Having workers who are able to step up and do additional 23 tasks is one of the necessities of a well-run organization. 24 25 We cannot be at the mercy of one of our essential employees getting sick or quitting, and now we have 26 become an emergency contractor again either having to shut down operations or hiring someone in a 27 hurry at exorbitant costs to the organization. 28 29 Communicate the Direction and Goals Daily 30 31 There is simply no excuse for not holding daily meetings with your crews. 32 33 Call them crew huddles or tailgate meetings but do them - daily! And set goals for the project, the day, or 34 the week -- whatever makes the most sense for your company and workers -- but setting targets always 35 comes back to inspire and refocus your workers. 36 This is another practice that will reap benefits for your organization, it has been proven numerous times 37 that employees seek a sense of belonging and participating in the company decisions more than an 38 increase in pay. 39 40 Create a Work Culture that Enjoys and Laughs 41 42 The most successful contractors, of any size, are all construction companies that enjoy people, celebrate 43 successes, and laugh a lot. The people at these organizations, from the owner to the lowest-paid laborer, 44 are just plain fun to hang out with. You can see the good-natured ribbing the workers dish out to their 45 foreman and, especially, to the owner. 46 47 You can keep people a long time with you if they enjoy you, the company culture, and are allowed to 48 laugh a little. A good supervisor is not afraid of laughing with and sharing good moments with their 49 employees. You as one of those type of supervisors, should be familiar with every one of your 50 employees, call them by their first names, ask them about their family and kids. 51 52 Remember, employees are looking for a feeling of belonging! 53 54 Institute a Meaningful Incentive Plan 55 56 OK, we finally see money showing up. Sure, money is important and the contractor who can creatively 57 reward performance with good pay and some form of bonus money will keep better workers around 58 longer. 59Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 3 - Successful Business Practices Reicon Publishing, LLC 35 1 There are a multitude of incentive-based compensation approaches, and they can be overly simple to 2 more complicated. Whatever path you follow, just be sure that your workers understand what it takes to 3 make that extra dollar. Yes, pay them what they are worth. Evaluate each one of them and give them the 4 rewards they deserve. 5 6 Personal evaluation of an employee is possibly one of the best tools a supervisor possesses to 7 communicate with them. Do not reward borderline employees, evaluate them, and be honest with them 8 then you will see that the majority of them change their attitudes and work habits. Some others will leave, 9 but then you are possibly lucky they left, and you did not have to fire them. 10 11 The most favored performance bonus is for gains made above what was set as a goal. 12 13 ASSESS YOUR PRODUCTS/SERVICES 14 15 Are you confident that your product is of a high quality in design and production? 16 17 Are you confident that there is a market for it? 18 19 Are you confident that your potential customers will pay the price you calculate necessary to meet your 20 costs? 21 22 These are some of the questions a contractor should ask about the company products and services. 23 Superior quality, timely production, and customer satisfaction are the qualities a successful contractor 24 must possess. A reputable contractor will be recognized in the industry and will be allowed, at times, to 25 obtain projects where their bid is not the lowest among the other bidders. 26 27 Measuring Construction Quality 28 29 Measuring quality is essential to improvement. With measurements, you can see trends, problem areas, 30 and gains made through improvement efforts. 31 32 KNOW YOUR MARKET AND COMPETITORS 33 34 One of the first questions people ask about a new idea or venture is, ‚ÄúWho is the competition?‚Äù Who else 35 is doing this? The worst rookie mistake in the world is to answer, ‚ÄúNo one.‚Äù 36 37 There is always a competitor. Even if you cannot find anyone else who is doing what you are doing, 38 someone probably is thinking about it. And even if nobody is thinking about it, you‚Äôll always have indirect 39 competitors, including the status quo and your customers‚Äô thrift. 40 41 As an entrepreneur you should be aware of the market where you are operating. 42 ‚Ä¢ Where are your services in that market? 43 ‚Ä¢ How is your company reputation? 44 ‚Ä¢ Who are your competitors? 45 ‚Ä¢ How busy are they? 46 ‚Ä¢ Do they have enough work that will allow you to increase your prices and get quality projects? 47 48 You will always have competition, and if you want to remain in business, you better get to know them! 49 50 It is essential that you know your market, as without this knowledge you cannot plan your route to market 51 or the means of promotion you will use to inform your customers of your existence. 52 53 It is also essential that you have a comprehensive knowledge of your competitors, as this knowledge will 54 allow you to distinguish what the market will stand plus identify the gaps in the market. 55 56 Market, being the geographical area as well as the type of services you are offering to the public, is an 57 essential part of being a successful contractor. By having a good knowledge of both market and 58 competitors you can develop a more effective marketing effort and successful costing and pricing 59 strategies.Chapter 3 - Successful Business Practices Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 36 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 2 One way in which to obtain knowledge is to join trade associations where you will be in touch not only 3 with competitors, but also with material suppliers, engineers, etc. who are familiar with the construction 4 environment and their input may be of great value. 5 6 Think about competition in three ways: 7 8 Barriers to entry 9 ‚Ä¢ Competition often means that there is a barrier to entry: someone or something that stands 10 between you and your customers. Facing a barrier to entry does not necessarily mean that 11 another person or company is doing exactly what you are or that it has a specific barrier such as 12 a patent that impedes your entry. 13 ‚Ä¢ If a customer‚Äôs needs are being met, there is a barrier. 14 ‚Ä¢ Always ask, ‚ÄúWhat barriers to entry will I have to surmount?‚Äù 15 ‚Ä¢ How is the customer‚Äôs need being met right now? 16 17 Differentiation 18 ‚Ä¢ Failing to differentiate and set up barriers to competitors and imitators inevitably leads to profit 19 erosion. In this race to the bottom, the most efficient producers and the least profitable 20 competitors drive down margins to push out their competition. 21 22 Resources 23 ‚Ä¢ Competitors are not just a problem; they are a great source of information and ideas. 24 ‚Ä¢ The larger theme is the ordeal of what could be called commodity hell. The place where 25 executives find themselves when they cannot convince customers that their widgets or services 26 are better than those of their ever-burgeoning competitors. All they can say is, ‚ÄúYep, we have 27 them too.‚Äù 28 29 Failing to differentiate will eventually land you in commodity hell. 30 31 COSTING AND PRICING 32 33 Costing and pricing are the most difficult tasks you need to address. 34 35 It is imperative that you know your break-even point: the number of units sold that will cover the costs of 36 your raw materials, overhead, and production time. Only when you know this figure (plus that of your 37 competitors), will you be comfortable in the knowledge of how high or low your price may go. 38 39 Calculating your break-even point is one of the most useful tools in the costing and bidding of your 40 operations. Your bookkeeper should be able to help you on that task. You should also know what your 41 different mark-up percentages for overhead are, profit. 42 43 How to Cost your Products 44 45 Be sure to price your work for the actual costs you will incur at the time you produce it. 46 47 If labor and material costs are expected to go up dramatically before the end of the year, allow for those 48 increases while preparing the cost of your products. 49 50 If you like the idea of an extended backlog of work, find a way to protect your planned profits from 51 escalating costs of material and labor. 52 53 During the good economic times, there is an abundance of work, so many sins go unnoticed and if you 54 are making money you might not bother to correct those sins. 55 56 You should have bidding policies for both types of economies: thriving and depressed. 57 58 If you must reduce your prices, do not reduce your actual costs. That should never be done. Play with 59 your profit markups and non-cash fixed overhead.Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 3 - Successful Business Practices Reicon Publishing, LLC 37 1 2 Be careful to watch your overhead expenses. Many construction businesses failed during the recession 3 because they could not cover the cost of the overhead they were committed to. 4 5 Know the cost of doing more business before you do more business, so you can use the right markup to 6 price your jobs profitably. 7 8 Before you increase your overhead costs, test the marketplace you plan to work in to make sure you can 9 sell at the increased pricing you will need and can sell enough work at that price. 10 11 Consider if you are in the right market to do business, but also if you have the right marketing and sales 12 skills in place. 13 14 Regardless of the type of economy in the industry, you should keep your overhead expenses low, 15 specially your fixed expenses. 16 17 Remember, those fixed expenses will be with you even in times when you have no backlog. 18 19 If you are planning on expansion or trying new services, before you increase your capital expenditures, 20 test the market to see if it warrants the additional expenditures. 21 22 Price your new products or services and test them in the marketplace to see the effect of the new 23 operation in your company wellbeing. 24 25 RISK MANAGEMENT 26 27 Risks are events that can adversely affect the successful outcome of a project or the company. Some of 28 those risks include: 29 ‚Ä¢ Staff lacking the technical skills to perform the work 30 ‚Ä¢ Hardware not delivered on time 31 ‚Ä¢ Infractions of some of the statutes causing down time 32 33 Risks will vary for each company, but the main risks to an organization or project must be identified as 34 soon as possible. Plans must be made to avoid the risk, or, if the risk cannot be avoided, to mitigate the 35 risk to lessen its impact if it occurs. This is known as risk management. 36 37 People associate risk management with insurance. To some extent, that is correct, but there are some 38 risks that cannot be insured by obtaining an insurance policy. Some of the risks facing a construction 39 company are mentioned below. We should identify them and try to correct them before they happen. 40 41 Assign risk factors to those unforeseen events and assign priorities based on those factors, with high risk 42 factors being the most severe ones. Remember risks that are allowed to remain unchecked could spell 43 disaster for any company. 44 45 You do not manage all risks because there could be too many and not all risks have the same impact. 46 ‚Ä¢ Identify all risks 47 ‚Ä¢ Estimate the likelihood of each risk occurring (1 = not likely, 2 = maybe likely, 3 = very likely) 48 ‚Ä¢ Estimate its impact on the project (1 = low, 2 = medium, 3 = high) 49 ‚Ä¢ Multiply the two numbers together to obtain the risk factor 50 51 High risk factors indicate the most severe risks. Manage the ten risks with the highest risk factors. 52 Constantly review risks and lookout for new ones since they have a habit of occurring at any moment. Not 53 managing risks effectively is a common reason why projects and companies fail. 54 KEEP GOOD RECORDS 55 56 There is no mystery to bookkeeping. It is nothing more than a filing system of the day-to-day transactions 57 of your business. If you do not record and understand the transactions, your business will control you 58 rather than you are controlling it. 59Chapter 3 - Successful Business Practices Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 38 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 In addition to bookkeeping, we are compelled by the different statutes governing our industry to maintain 2 records of certain activities for a determined period of time. 3 4 As mentioned previously, ‚Äúyou cannot do everything needed in your company.‚Äù If it is a task that you are 5 not comfortable with, get help, there is plenty of it out there, just look for it. 6 7 A good outside bookkeeping service will take care of keeping the books to follow the regulations dictated 8 by the governmental agencies and keep you informed of the financial status of your company. 9 10 Business Records Requirements; Address of Record; Service [F.S. 11 489.124] 12 13 This statute reads in part as follows: 14 ‚Ä¢ (1) All contractors who are registered or certified shall maintain complete financial and business records 15 for the immediately preceding 3 years. The business and financial records to be maintained shall include 16 minutes of corporate meetings, business contacts, telephone records, insurance policies, letters of 17 complaint, notices received from government entities, bank statements, cancelled checks, records of loan 18 documents, tax returns and all other business and financial records‚Ä¶ 19 20 Revenue Recognition 21 22 In the construction industry, there are two accepted methods to record income: 23 The percentage of completion method 24 The completed contract method 25 26 The Percentage of Completion Method 27 28 In the percentage of completion method, income is generally recognized when work is performed and 29 recorded when statements are mailed. 30 31 Contractors sometimes receive payments for work not yet performed or only partially completed. The 32 portion of income above the degree of completion is said to be unearned in the accounting sense. It is not 33 earned and should not be recorded as income until the work is done. 34 35 When you receive cash for work that is only partially complete, you have to separate out the unearned 36 portion of income and assign it to a special account. That sum will be recognized as income later, after it 37 is earned. Then you remove it from the special account. 38 That special account, since it will reflect money received from the client that has not been earned, shall 39 be treated as a liability. 40 This is known as percentage of completion accounting because you recognize as income only that 41 portion of money received, or charges billed for work that is completed. 42 43 With this method, your financial statements give a fairly accurate picture of your financial position, 44 including expected profits or losses. 45 46 The advantage of the percentage of completion method is that income and the related costs, expenses, 47 and profits are recognized and reported as the job progresses. 48 49 The disadvantage of the percentage of completion method is that all income, costs, expenses, and profits 50 are only estimates until the job is completed. 51 52 Completed Contract Method 53 54 Recognizing income by the completed contract method has the advantage of exactness. 55 56 There is no need to estimate the degree of completion because all income is recognized only when the 57 job is 100% finished. 58 59 This method is always used for small jobs, such as one-time work, material sales, isolated minorFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 3 - Successful Business Practices Reicon Publishing, LLC 39 1 contracts, and any other job with a short duration. 2 3 Payment for such work is usually in one installment. 4 5 You can use the completed contract method when you will complete the project within two years from the 6 contract date, and only if your annual gross receipts are under ten million dollars. 7 8 Discuss these methods with your accountant to determine which method is best suited for your company. 9 10 Preparing a Cash Budget 11 12 A cash budget must reflect business realities, since you use it to chart the future course of your operation. 13 There are two principal ways to prepare a cash budget or forecast: 14 ‚Ä¢ The cash movement method 15 ‚Ä¢ The source and application of funds method 16 17 The Cash Movement Method 18 19 The cash movement method involves budgeting only the flow of actual cash in and out of your business. 20 This method is especially valuable for builders who have wide variations in their business volume from 21 month-to-month. Such variation usually follows a predictable pattern. 22 23 Budgeting by the cash movement method lets you handle directly the cash flow changes that occur 24 because of monthly volume variations. 25 26 The Source and Application of Funds Method 27 28 The source and application of funds method is more precise than the cash movement method. It assumes 29 that you will attain a certain level of net income. Then it adjusts for non-cash changes to arrive at the net 30 increase or decrease in working capital. 31 32 Our recommendation is to discuss these methods with your accountant to determine which one is the 33 most beneficial for your company to utilize. 34 35 Cash Handling Problems and Solutions 36 37 In this section, we describe some of the problems that employers face with some employees whose 38 responsibility is to handle cash and collections. At the same time, we discuss some of the procedures 39 (solutions) that could be implemented to avoid them. 40 41 Lapping 42 43 Problem: Lapping is when cash is taken from the business by moving outstanding balances from one 44 customer account to another. The thief can write off the amount of cash taken as a bad debt, or they can 45 adjust the amount as a reconciling item to an out-of-balance system. 46 Solution: The way to prevent lapping is to insist on monthly accounts receivable aging lists of all 47 outstanding accounts. Compare this total to a reconciled accounts receivable total.Chapter 3 - Successful Business Practices Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 40 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 Unofficial Borrowing 2 3 Problem: An employee can take cash for periodic ‚Äúemergencies‚Äù and intend to pay back what they 4 borrow someday. However, the employee is often tempted to take more and more until it becomes 5 impossible to repay. This becomes unofficial borrowing. 6 Solution: Establish office rules on petty cash and stringent control on all funds. Carefully monitor your 7 employees who have access to checking and savings accounts, and cash awaiting deposit. 8 9 Failing to Record Cash Sales 10 11 Problem: Sales made for cash are only reported if the sale is recorded. An employee who receives the 12 cash and records the sale has the opportunity to pocket the whole amount by failing to record the cash 13 sale. 14 Solution: 15 ‚Ä¢ Set up an effective method of accepting cash 16 ‚Ä¢ Use invoices for billings and orders, and fill out sales receipts for cash sales 17 ‚Ä¢ File a copy of the invoice or receipt with the cash when it is received 18 ‚Ä¢ Keep and be able to justify all voided receipts and invoices 19 20 Falsifying or Taking Discount Amounts 21 22 Problem: Falsifying or taking discount amounts is when an employee makes a purchase, gets a receipt 23 for the net amount, and then pockets the discount amount. Another way is when a bill has been paid in 24 full, but the vendor sends a refund for the discount and the employee pockets that amount. 25 Solution: 26 ‚Ä¢ Know the discount terms of your suppliers. 27 ‚Ä¢ Implement a monthly discount summary so that you can verify the terms used by your employees against 28 those quoted by your suppliers. 29 30 Taking Payments and Writing off Accounts as Bad Debts 31 32 Problem: Taking payments and writing off accounts as bad debts is when an employee receives a 33 payment for a small invoice, pockets the money, and then writes that amount in accounts receivable as a 34 bad debt. 35 Solution: 36 ‚Ä¢ Approve all write-offs yourself 37 ‚Ä¢ Never delegate this job to others 38 ‚Ä¢ Prepare the bad debt analysis yourself for greater security 39 Financial Statements 40 41 Financial statements summarize your operation‚Äôs activity for a certain period, usually a month, quarter, or 42 year, and show its status at the end of that period. 43 44 There are three distinct financial statements: 45 ‚Ä¢ Balance sheet 46 ‚Ä¢ Income statement 47 ‚Ä¢ Statement of cash flow 48 49 Balance Sheet 50 51 The balance sheet is a summary of the existing conditions of the business. It lists the assets (things you 52 own), liabilities (things you owe), and net worth of the business as of a given date.Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 3 - Successful Business Practices Reicon Publishing, LLC 41 1 Income Statement 2 3 The income statement summarizes business operations within a time period. The ending date of that 4 period is always the same as the closing date on the balance sheet. 5 6 The income statement is broken down into these standard divisions: 7 ‚Ä¢ Sales - Revenues 8 ‚Ä¢ Expenses - Costs 9 ‚Ä¢ Net income from operations 10 11 Statement of Cash Flows 12 13 The statement of cash flows is a summary of the sources and uses of cash ‚Äì where it came from and 14 where it went - for the same period covered by the income statement. 15 16 Funds are generally from the following sources: 17 ‚Ä¢ Cash basis net income 18 ‚Ä¢ Sale of assets 19 ‚Ä¢ Increase in liabilities 20 ‚Ä¢ Decrease of other assets 21 22 The application or uses of funds include the purchase of long-term assets (equipment), the decrease of 23 liabilities, and the payment of taxes. 24 25 Any time you change the sources or applications of your funds, you affect your current assets and 26 liabilities. 27 28 Tips 29 30 You are a link in the chain of the business; form good relationships with other links. 31 32 Be professional. You are the main asset of your business. 33 34 Indecisiveness wastes time. Gain knowledge and make informed decisions. 35 36 Getting finance is not easy. Acquire it wisely. Spend it wisely. 37 38 You may have an excellent product but, if you are not getting adequately paid for it, you are not in 39 business. 40 41 Research the market. Know your competitors. 42 43 Do what you say and say what you do. 44 45 Implement successful business practices.Chapter 3 - Successful Business Practices Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 42 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 Primary Business Functions 2 3 Any business has three primary functions as displayed below. 4 Primary Business Functions Administration Production Sales Policies Resources Demand Methods Capacity Close Records Self-Correction Service Compliance Delivery Controls 5 6 Final Thought 7 8 ‚ÄúOrganizational structure is the oldest and most thoroughly studied area in management ‚Ä¶ we have 9 learned that organization does not start with structure but with building blocks; ‚Ä¶ that each enterprise 10 needs to design around the key activities appropriate to its mission‚Ä¶‚Äù 11 Peter F. Additional effects include: 35 36 ‚Ä¢ It removes the requirement that all delinquent status licensees, licensed by the DBPR or a board 37 in accordance with F.S. 455 must apply for reinstatement with a complete application, and 38 instead, the bill requires boards, or the DBPR if there is no board, to adopt by rule a process for 39 reinstating applicants with void licenses. 40 ‚Ä¢ It removes the requirement that engineers obtain a separate license (certificate of authorization) 41 for their engineering firm. 42 ‚Ä¢ It requires a licensed engineer to qualify an engineering firm without paying a separate licensing 43 fee. 44 ‚Ä¢ For a project that is wholly or partially funded by the department and administered by a local 45 governmental entity, the design services and construction engineering and inspection services 46 may not be performed by the same entity. 47 ‚Ä¢ It provides an additional pathway for licensure as a professional engineer after receiving an 48 engineering technology degree. 49 ‚Ä¢ It requires applicants for licensure to submit proof of being 18 years of age or older. 50 ‚Ä¢ It requires the Board of Professional Engineers to extend certain deadlines for granting or 51 denying a license after an applicant fails to appear before the board. 52 ‚Ä¢ It provides a temporary ‚Äúregistration‚Äù instead of a temporary ‚Äúcertificate‚Äù or ‚Äúlicense‚Äù for out-of- 53 state engineering businesses. 54 ‚Ä¢ It requires successor engineers to assume full responsibility when relying on the work, findings, or 55 recommendations of an engineer who previously sealed such documents and releases the 56 original engineer from liability for prior work assumed by the successor engineer. 57 ‚Ä¢ It includes repair and restoration works in which a structural system or structural loading of a 58 building is modified.
