Such application must be made either 7 when the license in another state or territory is active or within 2 years after such license was last active. 8 9 2021 Legislative Updates 10 11 HB 401 ‚Äì Florida Building Code 12 13 As it pertains to the administration of the Building Code, the bill: 14 ‚Ä¢ Allows a substantially affected person to petition the Commission for a non-binding advisory opinion 15 on whether a local government regulation is an improper amendment to the Building Code, and 16 establishes a process for such petitions; 17 ‚Ä¢ Allows the Commission to issue an ‚Äúerrata to the code‚Äù to list demonstrated errors in provisions 18 ‚Ä¢ contained within the Building Code; 19 ‚Ä¢ Requires the Commission to adopt rules for approving product evaluation entities in addition to the 20 ones already listed and approved in current law; 21 ‚Ä¢ Clarifies that the Commission may suspend product evaluation entities; 22 ‚Ä¢ Clarifies that local government entities may use private providers for their own construction projects; 23 ‚Ä¢ Provides that a local government may use excess funds generated by Building Code enforcement for 24 the construction of a building or structure that houses the local government‚Äôs building department or 25 provides training programs for building officials, inspectors, or plans examiners; 26 ‚Ä¢ Provides that excess funds used to construct such a building or structure must be designated for such 27 purpose by the local government and may not be carried forward for more than four years; and 28 ‚Ä¢ Prohibits a local government from requiring a contract between a builder and an owner as a condition 29 to apply for or obtain a building permit. 30 ‚Ä¢ Specifies that a local government may not use preliminary maps issued by the Federal Emergency 31 Management Agency for any law, ordinance, rule, or other measure that has the effect of imposing 32 land use changes or permits. 33 ‚Ä¢ The bill makes several changes to current law pertaining to licensed individuals providing private 34 building inspection services, known as ‚Äúprivate providers.‚Äù Current law allows contractors and 35 property owners to hire licensed building code administrators, engineers, and architects to review 36 building plans, perform building inspections, and prepare certificates of completion. The bill makes 37 the following changes to the private provider statute: 38 ‚Ä¢ Expressly authorizes private providers to conduct virtual building inspections. 39 ‚Ä¢ Allows private providers to submit various inspection forms, records, and reports electronically to local 40 building departments and utilize electronic signatures. 41 ‚Ä¢ Allows private providers to conduct ‚Äúsingle-trade inspections,‚Äù as defined in the bill. 42 ‚Ä¢ Authorizes private providers to conduct emergency inspection services. 43 ‚Ä¢ Additionally, the bill expressly authorizes local governments and school districts to use private 44 providers for public works projects and improvements to any building or structure. 45 ‚Ä¢ Finally, the bill amends the Community Planning Act to prohibit local governments from adopting land 46 development regulations that regulate specific building design elements (such as exterior color and 47 cladding, ornamentation, styling of windows and doors, etc.) for single- and two-family dwellings. 48 However, certain exceptions are provided that allow local governments to regulate such building 49 design elements when: The dwelling is a historic property or located in a historic district, a community 50 redevelopment area, or a planned unit development or master planned community. 51 52 Except as otherwise expressly provided in this act, this act shall take effect July 1, 2021. 53 54 F.S 163.3202 Land development regulations.‚Äî 55 56 Present subsection (5) of section 163.3202, Florida Statutes, is redesignated as subsection (6), and aFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter - 4 - The Principles of Florida Statutes & Rules Reicon Publishing, LLC 61 1 new subsection (5) is added to that section, to read: 2 3 (5)(a) Land development regulations relating to building design elements may not be applied to a single- 4 family or two-family dwelling unless: 5 6 1. A local 55 government may adopt technical amendments that address local needs if: 56 57 (4)(l) If a local government adopts a regulation, law, ordinance, policy, amendment, or land use or zoning 58 provision without using the process established in this subsection, and a substantially affected personChapter - 4 - The Principles of Florida Statutes & Rules Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 62 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 considers such regulation, law, ordinance, policy, amendment, or land use or zoning provision to be a 2 technical amendment to the Florida Building Code, then the substantially affected person may submit a 3 petition to the commission for a nonbinding advisory opinion. If a substantially affected person submits a 4 request in accordance with this paragraph, the commission shall issue a nonbinding advisory opinion 5 stating whether or not the commission interprets the regulation, law, ordinance, policy, amendment, or 6 land use or zoning provision as a technical amendment to the Florida Building Code. As used in this 7 paragraph, the term "local government" means a county, municipality, special district, or political 8 subdivision of the state. 9 10 1. Requests to review a local government regulation, law, ordinance, policy, amendment, or land 11 use or zoning provision may be initiated by any substantially affected person. A substantially 12 affected person includes an owner or builder subject to the regulation, law, ordinance, policy, 13 amendment, or land use or zoning provision, or an association of owners or builders having 14 members who are subject to the regulation, law, ordinance, policy, amendment, or land use or 15 zoning provision. 16 17 2. In order to initiate a review, a substantially affected person must file a petition with the 18 commission. The commission shall adopt a form for the petition and directions for filing, which 19 shall be published on the Building Code Information System. The form shall, at a minimum, 20 require the following: 21 22 a. The name of the local government that enacted the regulation, law, ordinance, policy, 23 amendment, or land use or zoning provision. 24 25 b. The name and address of the local government's general counsel or administrator. 26 27 c. The name, address, and telephone number of the petitioner; the name, address, and telephone 28 number of the petitioner's representative, if any; and an explanation of how the petitioner's 29 substantial interests are being affected by the regulation, law, ordinance, policy, amendment, or 30 land use or zoning provision. 31 32 d. A statement explaining why the regulation, law, ordinance, policy, amendment, or land use or 33 zoning provision is a technical amendment to the Florida Building Code, and which provisions of 34 the Florida Building Code, if any, are being amended by the regulation, law, ordinance, policy, 35 amendment, or land use or zoning provision. 36 37 3. The petitioner shall serve the petition on the local government's general counsel or 38 administrator by certified mail, return receipt requested, and send a copy of the petition to the 39 commission, in accordance with the commission's published directions. The local government 40 shall respond to the petition in accordance with the form by certified mail, return receipt 41 requested, and send a copy of its response to the commission, within 14 days after receipt of the 42 petition, including Saturdays, Sundays, and legal holidays. 43 44 4. Upon receipt of a petition that meets the requirements of this paragraph, the commission shall 45 publish the petition, including any response submitted by the local government, on the Building 46 Code Information System in a manner that allows interested persons to address the issues by 47 posting comments. 48 49 5. Before issuing an advisory opinion, the commission shall consider the petition, the response, 50 and any comments posted on the Building Code Information System. The commission may also 51 provide the petition, the response, and any comments posted on the Building Code Information 52 System to a technical advisory committee and may consider any recommendation provided by 53 the technical advisory committee. The commission shall issue an advisory opinion stating 54 whether the regulation, law, ordinance, policy, amendment, or land use or zoning provision is a 55 technical amendment to the Florida Building Code within 30 days after the filing of the petition, 56 including Saturdays, Sundays, and legal holidays. The commission shall publish its advisory 57 opinion on the Building Code Information System and in the Florida Administrative Register. The 58 commission's advisory opinion is nonbinding and is not a declaratory statement under s. 120.565. 59 60 (5) Notwithstanding subsection (4), counties and municipalities may adopt by ordinance an administrative 61 or technical amendment to the Florida Building Code relating to flood resistance in order to implement theFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter - 4 - The Principles of Florida Statutes & Rules Reicon Publishing, LLC 63 1 National Flood Insurance Program or incentives. Suspension and revocation is 29 governed by s. 120.60 and the uniform rules of procedure. 30 31 HB 667 - Building Inspections 32 33 Current law requires local governments to enforce the Building Code and issue building permits. Current 34 law also requires state agencies, state universities, Florida College System institutions, and public-school 35 districts to enforce the Building Code in certain situations. It is unlawful for a person or corporation to 36 construct, alter, repair, or demolish a building without obtaining a permit from the enforcing agency. 37 Construction work that requires a building permit requires inspections to ensure the work complies with 38 the Building Code. 39 40 The bill authorizes any government entity with the authority to enforce the Building Code to perform virtual 41 building inspections, with the exception of certain structural inspections. The bill defines ‚Äúvirtual 42 inspection‚Äù as an inspection that uses visual or electronic aids to allow a building official or inspector to 43 perform an inspection without having to be physically present at the job site during the inspection. 44 45 The bill also requires local building code enforcement agencies to allow requests for inspections to be 46 submitted to the local agency electronically via e-mail, electronic form, or mobile application. 47 48 Finally, the bill requires a building code enforcement agency to refund 10 percent of the permit and 49 inspection fees if: 50 51 The inspector or building official determines the work, which requires the permit, fails an inspection; and 52 The inspector or building official fails to provide a reason that is based on compliance with the Building 53 Code, the Florida Fire Prevention Code, or local ordinance, indicating why the work failed the inspection 54 within 5 business days. 55 56 This act shall take effect July 1, 2021 57 58 125.56 Enforcement and amendment of the Florida Building Code and the Florida Fire Prevention 59 Code; inspection fees; inspectors; etc.‚Äî 60 61 Paragraph (f) is added to subsection (4) of section 125.56, Florida Statutes, to read:Chapter - 4 - The Principles of Florida Statutes & Rules Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 66 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 2 4) (f) A county that issues building permits must allow requests for inspections to be submitted 3 electronically to the county building department. However, the exception for local government licensing imposed by a local government expires July 58 1, 2023. Local government occupational licensing requirements in place by January 1, 2021, may not be 59 increased or modified thereafter. 60Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter - 4 - The Principles of Florida Statutes & Rules Reicon Publishing, LLC 67 1 The bill specifically prohibits local governments from requiring a license for a person whose job scope 2 does not substantially correspond to that of a contractor or journeyman type licensed by the Construction 3 Industry Licensing Board, within the Department of Business and Professional Regulation. It specifically 4 precludes local governments from requiring a license for: painting, flooring, cabinetry, interior remodeling, 5 driveway or tennis court installation, handyman services, decorative stone, tile, marble, granite, or 6 terrazzo installation, plastering, stuccoing, caulking, and canvas awning and ornamental iron installation. 7 8 The bill authorizes counties and municipalities to issue journeyman licenses in the plumbing, pipe fitting, 9 mechanical, and HVAC trades, as well as the electrical and alarm system trades, which is the current 10 practice by counties and municipalities. As a result of this authorization in general law, local journeyman 11 licensing is excepted from the preemption of local licensing to the state under the bill. 12 13 This act shall take effect July 1, 2021 14 15 163.211 Licensing of occupations preempted to state.‚Äî 16 17 Section 163.211, Florida Statutes, is created to read: 18 19 (1) DEFINITIONS.-As used in this section: 20 (a) "Licensing" means any training, education, test, certification, registration, or license that is 21 required for a person to perform an occupation in addition to any associated fee. 22 (b) "Local government" means a county, municipality, special district, or political subdivision of the 23 state. 24 (c) "Occupation" means a paid job, profession, work, line of work, trade, employment, position, post, 25 career, field, vocation, or craft. 26 27 (2) PREEMPTION OF OCCUPATIONAL LICENSING TO THE STATE.- The licensing of occupations is 28 expressly preempted to the state and this section supersedes any local government licensing requirement 29 of occupations with the exception of the following: 30 (a) Any local government that imposed licenses on occupations before January 1, 2021. However, 31 any such local government licensing of occupations expires on July 1, 2023. 32 (b) Any local government licensing of occupations authorized by general law. 33 34 (3) EXISTING LICENSING LIMIT.-A local government that licenses occupations and retains such 35 licensing as set forth in paragraph (2)(a) may not impose additional licensing requirements on that 36 occupation or modify such licensing. 37 38 (4) LOCAL LICENSING NOT AUTHORIZED.‚ÄîLocal licensing of an occupation that is not authorized 39 under this section or otherwise authorized by general law does not apply and may not be enforced. 40 41 489.117 Registration; specialty contractors.‚Äî 42 43 Paragraph (a) of subsection (4) of section 489.117, Florida Statutes, is amended to read: 44 45 (4)(a) A person holding a local license whose job scope does not substantially correspond to either the 46 job scope of one of the contractor categories defined in s. 489.105(3)(a)-(o), or the job scope of one of 47 the certified specialty contractor categories established by board rule, is not required to register with the 48 board to perform contracting activities within the scope of such specialty license. The 31 Florida Building Codes Act provides a mechanism for the uniform adoption, updating, interpretation, and 32 enforcement of a single, unified state building code. The Florida Building Code (Building Code) is the 33 statewide building code for all construction in the state and must be applied, administered, and enforced 34 uniformly and consistently from jurisdiction to jurisdiction. 35 36 Local governments enforce the Building Code within their jurisdictions, primarily by conducting 37 inspections and issuing building permits to authorize construction. It is unlawful to construct, alter, repair, 38 or demolish a building without obtaining a building permit. 39 40 The bill makes various changes to the ways in which local enforcement agencies receive and process 41 building permit applications. Specifically, the bill requires local enforcement agencies to: 42 Allow building permit applications, including payments, attachments, drawings, and other documents, to 43 be submitted electronically. 44 Post the current status of every building permit application received on its website. 45 Post the agency‚Äôs procedures for reviewing, processing, and approving building permit applications on its 46 website. 47 Review additional information for an application for a development permit or development order within a 48 certain time-period. 49 Allow building permit applicants 10 business days to correct an application for a single-family residential 50 dwelling that was initially denied by the local enforcement agency. 51 Reduce permit fees by specified amounts after failing to meet statutory deadlines for reviewing certain 52 building permit applications. 53 54 Finally, the bill prohibits government entities, which enforce the Building Code, from requiring a copy of a 55 contractor‚Äôs contract with owners, subcontractors, or suppliers in order to obtain a building permit for 56 projects on commercial property.Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter - (New) 22 23 Circumstances which may be considered for the purposes of mitigation or aggravation of penalty 24 shall include the following: 25 (1) Monetary or other damage to the licensee‚Äôs customer, in any way associated with the violation, 26 which damage the licensee has not relieved, as of the time the penalty is to be assessed. (This 27 provision shall not be given effect to the extent it would contravene federal bankruptcy law.) 28 (2) Actual job-site violations of building codes, or conditions exhibiting gross negligence, 29 incompetence, or misconduct by the licensee, which have not been corrected as of the time the 30 penalty is being assessed. 31 (3) The danger to the public. 32 (4) The number of complaints filed against the licensee. 33 (5) The length of time the licensee has practiced. 34 (6) The actual damage, physical, economic, or otherwise, caused by the licensee‚Äôs violation. 35 (7) The deterrent effect of the penalty imposed. 36 (8) The effect of the penalty upon the licensee‚Äôs livelihood. 37 (9) Any efforts at rehabilitation. 38 (10) Any other mitigating circumstances. 39 40 61G6 ‚Äì Electrical Contractors Licensing Board (ECLB) 41 42 61G6-7.001 Specialty Electrical Contractors 43 44 (6) Two-Way Radio Communications Enhancement Systems Specialty. Examples of acceptable courses include 13 philosophy, religion, history, literature, fine arts, sociology, psychology, political science, anthropology, 14 economics, (micro and macro), professional ethics, and social responsibility. Examples of other general 15 education courses deemed acceptable include management (such as organizational behavior), 16 accounting, written and oral communications, business, and law. English and foreign language courses in literature 21 and civilization may be considered in this area. Courses that instill cultural values are acceptable, while 22 routine exercises of personal craft are not. Courses in this area shall have their roots in mathematics and 28 basic sciences but carry knowledge further toward creative application of engineering principles. 29 Examples of approved engineering science courses are mechanics, thermodynamics, heat transfer, 30 electrical and electronic circuits, materials science, transport phenomena, engineering economics, and 31 computer science (other than computer programming skills). Courses in engineering design stress the 32 establishment of objectives and criteria, synthesis, analysis, construction, testing, and evaluation. 33 Graduate-level engineering courses may be included to fulfill curricular requirements in this area. A 34 maximum of six credit hours will be granted for thesis, dissertation, special topics and independent study 35 at any level. Graphics, surveying, or engineering technology courses will not be considered to meet 36 engineering science and design requirements. Building Code Chapter 1 ‚Äì Administration. 31 32 The First Step 33 34 The Building Official has the authority to approve products that he/she feels meet the intent and 35 requirements of the Code . 36 37 [A]104.9 Approved materials and equipment. 38 39 Materials, equipment, and devices approved by the building official shall be constructed and installed in 40 accordance with such approval. 41 42 [A]104.9.1 Used materials and equipment. 43 44 The use of used materials that meet the requirements of this code for new materials is permitted. Used 45 equipment and devices shall not be reused unless approved by the building official. 46 47 The Building Official Approval 48 49 If the Building Official is comfortable with the information provided, the BO may approve the use. 50 51 If the Building Official is not familiar with the product, is not comfortable with the design or calculations, 52 then 53 54 The Building Official can go to Step 2 and/ or 3. 55 56 57 The Second Step 58Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 5 - Understanding Product Approval Reicon Publishing, LLC 83 1 The Building Official can request information documenting the product, the design, test information or 2 other information related to the product. 3 4 This information may be a test report from a recognized test lab. 5 6 These reports are items such as a Miami Dade Notice of Acceptance (NOA), an ICC Evaluation Services 7 (ICC ES) report, a Texas Department of Insurance (TDI) report, or 8 9 A rational Analysis of a previous or similar report about the product and the specific installation from an 10 expert, such as a Professional Engineer. 11 12 Most Building Officials are not experts in every field. 13 14 Most Building Officials are not practicing engineers. 15 16 Documentation and verification of products and their performance is generally required. 17 18 This information is readily available from the various testing entities and manufacturers. 19 20 Let us look at the statutory wording. 21 22 553.8425 - Local Product Approval. 23 24 1) For local product approval, products or systems of construction shall demonstrate compliance with 25 the structural windload requirements of the Florida Building Code through one of the following 26 methods: 27 28 a) A certification mark, listing, or label from a commission-approved certification agency indicating 29 that the product complies with the code; 30 31 b) A test report from a commission-approved testing laboratory indicating that the product tested 32 complies with the code; 33 34 c) A product-evaluation report based upon testing, comparative or rational analysis, or a 35 combination thereof, from a commission-approved product evaluation entity which indicates that 36 the product evaluated complies with the code; 37 38 d) A product-evaluation report or certification based upon testing or comparative or rational analysis, 39 or a combination thereof, developed and signed and sealed by a Florida professional engineer or 40 Florida registered architect, which indicates that the product complies with the code; 41 42 e) A statewide product approval issued by the Florida Building Commission; or 43 44 f) Designation of compliance with a prescriptive, material standard adopted by the commission by 45 rule under s. 553.842(15). 46 47 The Third Step 48 49 If Other Options Are Not Available - The third step is the Florida Product Approval System. 50 51 This was established to have a central clearing house of products that meet the Florida Building Code for 52 specific installations. 53 54 An Important Note! 55 56 No matter which system is used, just because a product has a test sheet or product approval number, the 57 use of that product must still meet the specific criteria of the installation. 58 This relates to design pressures, impact rating, fire resistance and attachment methods applicable to the 59 installation. 60 61 553.842 - Product evaluation and approvalChapter 5 - Understanding Product Approval Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 84 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 2 1) The commission shall adopt rules under ss. 120.536(1) and 120.54 to develop and implement a 3 product evaluation and approval system that applies statewide to operate in coordination with the 4 Florida Building Code. The commission may enter into contracts to provide for administration of the 5 product evaluation and approval system. The commission‚Äôs rules and any applicable contract may 6 provide that the payment of fees related to approvals be made directly to the administrator. Any fee 7 paid by a product manufacturer shall be used only for funding the product evaluation and approval 8 system. The product evaluation and approval system shall provide: 9 10 a) Appropriate promotion of innovation and new technologies. 11 12 b) Processing submittals of products from manufacturers in a timely manner. 13 14 c) Independent, third-party qualified and accredited testing and laboratory facilities, product 15 evaluation entities, quality assurance agencies, certification agencies, and validation entities. 16 17 d) An easily accessible product acceptance list to entities subject to the Florida Building Code. 18 19 e) Development of stringent but reasonable testing criteria based upon existing consensus 20 standards, when available, for products. 21 22 f) Long-term approvals, where feasible. State and local approvals will be valid until the 23 requirements of the code on which the approval is based change, the product changes in a 24 manner affecting its performance as required by the code, or the approval is revoked. However, 25 the commission may authorize by rule editorial revisions to approvals and charge a fee as 26 provided in this section. 27 28 g) Criteria for revocation of a product approval. 29 30 h) Cost-effectiveness. 31 32 Parenthesis 2 tells us that this system is designed to rely on national and international consensus 33 standards. This leads us back to the existing approval systems and to the published reports. 34 35 2) The product evaluation and approval system shall rely on national and international consensus 36 standards, whenever adopted by the Florida Building Code, for demonstrating compliance with 37 code standards. Other standards which meet or exceed established state requirements shall also 38 be considered. 39 40 The Commission Operation 41 42 In order to apply the law, the Commission established a rule for the application of the requirements . 43 44 An important note to be aware of is the Florida Product Approval System applies only to the envelope 45 and structural frame. Whether Prescriptive or Performance, 35 Inspections are required. 36 37 We will now look at those inspections which are mandatory to ensure compliance. 38 39 C104.2.1 Footing and foundation inspection. 40 41 Inspections associated with footings and foundations shall verify compliance with the code as to R-value, 42 location, thickness, depth of burial and protection of insulation as required by the code 43 and approved plans and specifications. 44 45 C104.2.2 Framing and rough-in inspection 46 47 Inspections at framing and rough-in shall be made before application of interior finish and shall verify 48 compliance with the code as to types of insulation and corresponding R-values and their correct location 49 and proper installation; fenestration properties (U-factor, SHGC and VT) and proper installation; and air 50 leakage controls as required by the code and approved plans and specifications. 51 52 C104.2.3 Plumbing rough-in inspection. 53 54 Inspections at plumbing rough-in shall verify compliance as required by the code and approved plans and 55 specifications as to types of insulation and corresponding R-values and protection; required controls; and 56 required heat traps. 57 C104.2.4 Mechanical rough-in inspection.Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 6 - Energy Conservation Volume Reicon Publishing, LLC 99 1 2 Inspections at mechanical rough-in shall verify compliance as required by the code and approved plans 3 and specifications as to installed HVAC equipment type and size; required controls, system insulation and 4 corresponding R-value; system and damper air leakage; and required energy recovery and economizers. 5 6 C104.2.5 Electrical rough-in inspection. 7 8 Inspections at electrical rough-in shall verify compliance as required by the code and approved plans and 9 specifications as to installed lighting systems, components and controls; and installation of an electric 10 meter for each dwelling unit. 11 12 C104.2.6 Final inspection. 13 14 The building shall have a final inspection and shall not be occupied until approved. The final inspection 15 shall include verification of the installation and proper operation of all required building controls, and 16 documentation verifying activities associated with required building commissioning have been conducted 17 and findings of noncompliance corrected. Same for Both Residential and Commercial. 45 46 C/R 101.4.2 Exempt buildings. 47 48 Buildings exempt from the provisions of the Florida Building Code, Energy Conservation, include existing 49 buildings except those considered renovated buildings, changes of occupancy type or previously 50 unconditioned buildings to which comfort conditioning is added. Exempt buildings include those specified 51 in Sections C/R101.4.2.1 through C/R101.4.2.4. 52 53 This definition provides an exemption to the requirements of the Energy Conservation volume. The 54 statutory language was incorporated into Florida law in 1992. It has been a part of the Energy Code and 55 then the Energy Conservation volume ever since. 56 57 58 This basically allows existing buildings undergoing repairs and alterations to utilize the codes in effect atFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 6 - Energy Conservation Volume Reicon Publishing, LLC 113 1 the time of construction to be applied if the cost of the repairs or alterations do not exceed 30% of the 2 assessed value of the structure (not including the land). 3 4 Repairing Portions and Systems 5 6 Chapter 5 of the Energy Conservation volume covers Existing Buildings. This is true in both the 7 Commercial and Residential sections. Similar requirements exist in the beginning of each. 8 9 We will look at the Commercial sections and then the Residential. 10 11 C501.1 Scope. 12 13 The provisions of this chapter shall control the alteration, repair, addition and change of occupancy of 14 existing buildings and structures. 15 16 While this section seems to apply to all work on existing buildings, remember the exemption for buildings 17 that are not considered ‚Äúrenovated‚Äù. 18 19 C501.2 Existing buildings. 20 21 Except as specified in this chapter, this code shall not be used to require the removal, alteration, or 22 abandonment of, nor prevent the continued use and maintenance of, an existing building or building 23 system lawfully in existence at the time of adoption of this code. 24 25 This section clearly helps us to understand the intent is to allow the continuation of existing buildings and 26 we are not requiring existing buildings to replace everything in order to continue their use. 27 28 Again, this is further information reinforcing that existing buildings may continue in a safe condition based 29 on the code they were constructed to. 30 31 C501.3 Maintenance. 32 33 Buildings and structures, and parts thereof, shall be maintained in a safe and sanitary condition. The owner or the owner‚Äôs authorized agent shall be responsible for the maintenance of 36 buildings and structures. The requirements of this chapter shall not provide the basis for removal or 37 abrogation of energy conservation, fire protection and safety systems and devices in existing structures. 38 39 C501.4 Compliance. 40 41 Alterations, repairs, additions, and changes of occupancy to, or relocation of, existing buildings and 42 structures shall comply with the provisions for alterations, repairs, additions and changes of occupancy or 43 relocation, respectively, in the Florida Building Code, Building; Florida Fire Prevention Code; Florida 44 Building Code, Fuel Gas; Florida Building Code, Mechanical; Florida Building Code, Plumbing; and NFPA 45 70. 46 47 C501.5 New and replacement materials. 48 49 Except as otherwise required or permitted by this code, materials permitted by the applicable code for 50 new construction shall be used. Does it mean Commercial applications need to comply as the Code changes? 41 42 No! 43 44 Let us look at Residential requirements. 45 46 R501.1.1 Additions, alterations, or repairs: General. 47 48 Additions, alterations, or repairs to an existing building, building system or portion thereof shall comply 49 with Section R502, R503 or R504. Unaltered portions of the existing building or building supply system 50 shall not be required to comply with this code. 51 52 R501.2 Existing buildings. 53 54 Except as specified in this chapter, this code shall not be used to require the removal, alteration, or 55 abandonment of, nor prevent the continued use and maintenance of, an existing building or building 56 system lawfully in existence at the time of adoption of this code. 57Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 6 - Energy Conservation Volume Reicon Publishing, LLC 115 1 R501.3 Maintenance. 2 3 Buildings and structures, and parts thereof, shall be maintained in a safe and sanitary condition. The owner or the owner‚Äôs authorized agent shall be responsible for the maintenance of 6 buildings and structures. The requirements of this chapter shall not provide the basis for removal or 7 abrogation of energy conservation, fire protection and safety systems and devices in existing structures. 8 9 R501.5 New and replacement materials. 10 11 Except as otherwise required or permitted by this code, materials permitted by the applicable code for 12 new construction shall be used. Like materials shall be permitted for repairs, provided hazards to life, 13 health or property are not created. Hazardous materials shall not be used where the code for new 14 construction would not permit their use in buildings of similar occupancy, purpose, and location. 15 16 There is much similarity between Commercial and Residential. 17 18 Repairs are not intended to require updates to meet the current requirements. 19 Repairs cannot make any portion less safe or less energy efficient than it was previously. 20 A repair is intended to take an item that is broken and make it work at least as efficiently as it did 21 previously. 22 23 Is Engineering, Paperwork and Calculations Required? 24 25 When is it required to provide engineering for a project? 26 27 ‚Ä¢ When it is new 28 ‚Ä¢ For additions 29 ‚Ä¢ For change of occupancy 30 31 If so, what documentation is needed? 32 33 ‚Ä¢ Computer printouts, especially for performance based. 34 ‚Ä¢ Residential ‚Äì Blower door test documentation. 35 36 Computer Software 37 38 Numerous programs are available to verify code compliance. The scope of 24 work includes the repair or replacement of existing equipment, any cleaning or equipment sanitizing that 25 requires at least a partial disassembling, excluding filter changes, and the installation of new pool/spa 26 equipment, interior refinishing, the reinstallation or addition of pool heaters, the repair or replacement of 27 all perimeter piping and filter piping, the repair of equipment rooms or housing for pool/spa equipment, 28 and the substantial or complete draining of a swimming pool, or hot tub or spa, for the purpose of repair 29 or renovation. 30 31 The problem is when the equipment to be installed, repaired, or replaced is connected and powered by 32 electric wires. 33 34 Who can disconnect/reconnect? 35 36 Is this within the scope of a Swimming Pool/Spa Contractor? 37 38 Is a Swimming Pool Contractor obligated to subcontract to an electrician? 39 40 The statute did not speak clearly to this. 41 42 What was allowed in one part of the state was not allowed in others based on interpretation of the same 43 statute. 44 45 Section 1.04 - The Empowering Legislation 46 47 The CILB has statutory authority to make changes to their rules. 48 49 F.S. 489.101 Purpose. 50 51 ‚Äù The Legislature deems it necessary in the interest of the public health, safety, and welfare to regulate 52 the construction industry.‚Äù 53Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Reicon Publishing, LLC 119 1 489.108 Rulemaking authority. 2 3 ‚Äù The board has authority to adopt rules pursuant to ss. 120.536(1) and 120.54 to implement the 4 provisions of this chapter conferring duties upon it.‚Äù 5 6 After many months and many meetings and much discussion between all the ‚Äústakeholders‚Äù the following 7 2 rules were developed and adopted: 61G4-12.011(16) and 61G4-18.001. 8 9 Section 1.05 - 61G4-12.011 (16) 10 11 This is the definition and part of the rules created by the Board. 12 16) Installation or repair: 13 14 ‚ÄúPursuant to section 489.105(3)(j)(k)(l), F.S., the term ‚Äúinstallation or repair‚Äù of pool/spa equipment, as it 15 pertains to swimming pool and spa electrical work, is defined as inclusive of installation, replacement, 16 disconnection or reconnection of power wiring on the load side of the dedicated existing electrical 17 disconnecting means. If installation, removal, replacement, or upgrading of this circuit is necessary, the 18 work shall be performed by a licensed electrical contractor only. Nothing in this paragraph shall be 19 deemed to restrict or limit in any manner the scope of work authorized by law of other contractor 20 classifications, or other professions.‚Äù 21 22 Purpose of rule change 23 24 To clear up ambiguity in F.S. 489.105 regarding installation & repair of existing pool equipment. 25 To align the Pool Contractors stated scope of work with what was being practiced in the field. 26 To give guidance to local building departments as to what license is appropriate to issue a permit to when 27 involving the installation & repairs of electrically powered swimming pool equipment. 28 To allow pool contractors to legally disconnect and reconnect wiring to existing pool equipment for the 29 purpose of servicing without unnecessarily raising the cost to the public. 30 31 This new rule became law and effective on 5/24/2017. 32 33 On load side of dedicated existing disconnecting means: 34 35 On the equipment side of the electrical current supply line. Downstream from electric current shutoff or 36 disconnect. 37 Disconnect be dedicated: 38 Single pole electric switch. 39 Timeclock. 40 Cartridge fuse box disconnect. 41 Circuit breaker (only for shutoff purposes, not to be repaired or replaced!!). 42 43 Section 1.06 - 61G4-158.001 44 45 As part of the negotiations and rule making process it was agreed that if swimming pool contractors are to 46 be allowed to perform minor electric work on pool equipment, some continuing education on the basics of 47 electrical maintenance & safety procedures be required. 48 49 Rule 61G4-18.001 (CE) was opened as a companion rule and amended to require swimming pool 50 contractors to complete 1 hour of CE course time on swimming pool electric topics. 51 52 The 1 hour is INCLUDED as part of the normal required 14 hours of CE every 2 years and does not 53 increase the CE course time requirement. 54 55 This rule became law on July 19, 2017, and will be enforced beginning with the CE renewal cycle 56 ending 8/31/2018. 57 58 This is in the FAC which is the Boards Rule. They have the same electrical requirements though! 53 Pool Not Permanent: This is a pool but NOT PERMANENT. It has different electrical requirements. 54 Kid Pools: Not a pool as there is no circulating system. It has no electrical requirements. 55 56 Section 2.03 - Corrosion & Electrical Systems 57 58 Swimming pool construction & maintenance should be concerned with each piece of equipmentFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Reicon Publishing, LLC 121 1 connected to the pool. 2 3 A swimming pool facility is an interconnected system of heaters, filters, pumps, drains and so much more, 4 which means that a swimming Pool Contractor needs to stay on top of all these devices to ensure a clean 5 & safe swimming environment. 6 7 Running a pool day after day places a great amount of strain on the devices that keep the water clean 8 and swimmers safe, and every pool manager should know just how corrosion can affect their facilities. 9 10 ‚ÄúCorrosion is the culprit and inspection are the only way to prevent or at least 11 control it.‚Äù 12 13 Whether constructing a new swimming pool or providing maintenance and repair of equipment, it is 14 important to choose the right materials to ensure adequate corrosion-resistance. In swimming pool 15 operations, equipment is under constant exposure to moistures and various pool chemicals, which are 16 highly corrosive. 17 18 Due to the corrosive properties of Chlorine, Muriatic Acid, salts and the weather itself the electrical 19 components of a pool are under constant attack. These system and components should be inspected 20 upon every visit whether for routine maintenance & servicing or repairing a specific part. 21 22 ‚ÄúCorrosion can take many forms, and correct selection of materials and finishes 23 is key to preventing each type. Follow these three steps when installing or 24 replacing electrical components: ‚Äú 25 26 Select materials according to their galvanic properties. Different metals and alloys have different 27 electrode potentials. When two different metals are electrically connected in the presence of an 28 electrolyte, such as seawater, the more active metal will become anodic ‚Äì losing electrons and increasing 29 its oxidation state in a process known as galvanic corrosion. Galvanic corrosion can be minimized by 30 selecting metals close together in the galvanic series. 31 32 Use protective coatings. Various finishes can be applied to help isolate metallic surfaces from the 33 surrounding corrosive environment. The most familiar example is ordinary paint applied to steel to prevent 34 rust, but there are many other methods including baked enamel, epoxy powder coat, and PVC coating. 35 36 Choose nonmetallic components. Manufacturers are increasingly offering nonmetallic products that are 37 completely impervious to the environments and substances that can corrode metals. 38 39 Section 2.04 - Impedance & Corrosion 40 41 What is impedance? 42 43 Think of the word: IMPEDE which means ‚Äúto hinder‚Äù. Simply put, impedance is a number measuring the 44 resistance an electric circuit or wire to the flow of an AC electrical current. 45 46 When impedance becomes too great for a given flow of AC electrical current the result is excessive heat 47 being built up in the wire or circuit. If this heat becomes too great there is an increasing chance of fire 48 being created. 49 50 Impedance and required AC current for an electrically powered pool component is the reason why certain 51 gauge wires are specified for the connect to and from electrical components. 52 53 The fact is that corrosion will many time increase the impedance of a wire or the connections of wires to 54 the components. 55 All electrical connection to pool equipment should be checked on a regular basis. If corrosion is setting 56 in, sometimes a simple cleaning and reconnecting is all that is needed. In more severe instances, the wire 57 or connection must be replaced. 58 59 A licensed pool professional should always perform a safety inspection of the electrically poweredChapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 122 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 components of the pool for safety issues much as a car mechanic will perform a safety inspection when a 2 car is in for an oil change. 3 4 Section 2.05 - Grounding vs. Bonding 5 6 What does ‚ÄúGrounding‚Äù mean? 7 8 The National Electrical Code (NFPA 70) defines the word ‚ÄúGround‚Äù as the earth. 9 We think of something being grounded as having a safety wire, the green conductor. It is never used 10 during normal operation; it only comes into play during a ground fault. 11 This conductor is used only in case of a ground fault and is intended only to carry current long enough to 12 clear an overcurrent device. The bonding conductor is always carrying voltage and/or current. 13 14 Ground to the earth. 15 16 Ground Fault: An unintentional, electrically conducting connection between an ungrounded conductor of 17 an electrical circuit and the normally non‚Äìcurrent-carrying conductors, metallic enclosures, metallic 18 raceways, metallic equipment, or earth. 19 Grounded (Grounding): Connected (connecting) to ground or to a conductive body that extends the 20 ground connection. 21 Grounded, Solidly: Connected to ground without inserting any resistor or impedance device. 22 Grounded Conductor: A system or circuit conductor that is intentionally grounded. 23 24 A ground fault is not a short circuit! 25 26 A short circuit is when the wires intended to be part of the circuit touch each other, and generally go 27 boom!! 28 A ground fault is when electricity goes through an unintended path, such as the case of the motor, or 29 you. The ground wire is used to carry to carry this current, referred to as fault or overcurrent, we hope, in 30 order to open the fuse or circuit breaker ahead of the circuit This is referred to as clearing the fault or 31 opening the overcurrent device. 32 A bond wire is always carrying voltage and current because it is making sure there is no difference of 33 potential between the connected items. That is why it is bigger, and it will protect you, if you, and your 34 customer, make sure it is in place, the right size and properly connected. 35 36 Section 2.06 - Why Do We Ground Items? 37 38 This is a ground fault. It is important you understand these concepts and theories. The topics below, 39 show what they are, and how a ground fault is being created, not a short circuit. 40 To clear ground faults. 41 Grounding is not related to a short circuit. 42 A ground fault is an unintended path. 43 A GFCI would protect this individual! 44 A breaker or fuse will not! 45Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Reicon Publishing, LLC 123 1 2 Section 2.07 - Why Do We Bond Items? 3 4 Bonding is one of the most important items around pools. All the metal around the electrical service 5 is bonded to the neutral for protection and ensures stability of the voltage once attached to earth. All 6 metal piping systems including water, metal ducts, building steel and numerous other items, are bonded 7 to prevent arcing, sparking, and limit shock hazards. Tying everything together prevents voltages 8 traveling between the various services, protecting the users of the systems. 9 10 It eliminates voltage gradients. 11 Very simply, for safety! 12 It eliminates voltage differences. 13 It is required by the governing Codes. 14 It prevents shock hazards from developing. 15 16 Bonding the Pump Motor 17 18 If not done, this is a blatant violation and life safety hazard! 19 It must be installed initially by electrician. 20 Pool contractors must inspect, maintain, and repair if necessary. 21 Critical to proper safety. 22 Pool contractors must check for integrity during every visit. 23 24 The importance of bonding and grounding in swimming pools, equipment, deck 25 and adjacent structures and cannot be overstated. 26 27 The grounded circuits of electrically powered pumps and equipment need to 28 have an effective return path from the equipment to the power source in 29 order to function properly. 30 31 In addition, non-current-carrying metallic components in a swimming pool 32 facility, such as structural steel, ladders, slides, diving boards and screen 33 enclosures need to be electrically interconnected so voltage potential cannot 34 exist between them. 35 36 Section 2.08 - The Mysterious GFCI 37 38 A GFCI is a simple device. It stands for Ground Fault Circuit Interrupter and will 39 not clear a short circuit. 40 41 It monitors the hot and neutral for any imbalance in current. The imbalance can be on the hot or neutral. 42 43 Often consumers do not understand them, and they also think it has an effect when someone is caught 44 between the hot and neutral. A GFCI ONLY detects ground faults and that needs to be stressed. A 45 ground fault is when EITHER the hot or neutral current is diverted from the circuit conductors through an 46 alternate path. 47 48 If for example, a person contacts the hot or neutral wire and conducts a flow of electricity to earth, the 49 current transformer shown in the diagram (shown on the next slide) senses a difference of electrical flow 50 between the hot and neutral. When this flow exceeds the 5-mA threshold, the device ‚Äútrips‚Äù and 51 disconnects the hot and neutral conductor from the circuit to protect the person. 52 53 Notice in the diagram, the test button simulates the same type of scenario previously mentioned and 54 should be tested once a month, according to the manufacturer's instructions. 55Chapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 124 Reicon Publishing, LLC 12 3 GFCI vs. AFCI 4 5 Ground Fault Circuit Interrupters (GFCI) help prevent burns, electric shocks, and electrocution. A 6 GFCI has sensors that measure the current going out and the current coming back in. Normally, the 7 current is balanced as it goes out and comes back in. 8 However, if the current is out of balance, something is wrong. The electric current has contacted a 9 human, or somewhere else it should not be. 10 11 The GFCI senses this and instantly shuts down the circuit, stopping the flow of electricity. Since water is 12 an excellent electric conductor, GFCIs are important in areas where water and electricity could meet, 13 such as bathrooms, kitchens, laundry rooms and garages. 14 15 GFCI vs. AFCI 16 17 Arc Fault Circuit Interrupters (AFCI) help prevent electric fires. An AFCI should never be installed in 18 place of or as a substitute for a GFCI. 19 20 Electricity can leak out of damaged or decaying wires and start a fire. These fires spread quickly in the 21 wiring behind walls. Electric fires cause more damage than other types of fire and are twice as deadly. 22 AFCIs sense that electricity is leaking from the electric system and shut electricity off before overheating 23 happens. 24 25 Basically, GFCIs prevent shocks, and AFCIs prevent fires. By law BOTH MUST be installed by a 26 qualified electrician. 27 28 Section 2.09 - Relevant Codes, Standards, & Definitions 29 30 Relevant Standards: NEC ‚Äì Article 680 31 32 This is the section of the electrical code that requires the bonding of metal items. This applies to motors, 33 lights, both in and out of the pool, metal windows, metal doors, screen enclosures and basically anything 34 metal that is in, on or around the pool that could possibly become energized. Yes, even the copper 35 shower located on the pool deck if it is within 5 feet. 36Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Reicon Publishing, LLC 125 1 680.26(B) Bonded Parts 2 3 ‚Ä¢ Conductive Pool Shell ÔÇ° Metal Fittings 4 ‚Ä¢ Perimeter Surfaces ÔÇ° Electrical Equipment 5 ‚Ä¢ Metallic Components ÔÇ° Fixed Metal Parts 6 ‚Ä¢ Underwater Lighting 7 8 7th Edition FBC - Residential: Chapters 42 & 45 9 10 ‚ÄúA ‚ÄòSwimming Pool Private‚Äô means any structure, located in a residential area, that is intended for 11 swimming or recreational bathing and contains water over 24 inches deep, including, but not limited to, 12 in-ground, above-ground, and on-ground swimming pools; hot tubs; and non-portable spas.‚Äù 13 14 Earlier we discussed what a pool might be. The Building Code has specific concerns for safety as 18 does the electrical code. Electrically, we need to be incredibly careful around bodies of water that contain 19 minerals, dissolved solids and other chemicals. 20 21 The electrical code rules when those regulations apply, and the building code governs when 22 those definitions apply. 23 24 A ‚Äúpublic swimming pool‚Äù or ‚Äúpublic pool‚Äù means a single watertight structure of concrete, masonry 25 or other approved materials that is located either indoors or outdoors, used for bathing or swimming by 26 humans, and filled with a filtered and disinfected water supply, together with buildings, appurtenances 27 and equipment used in connection therewith. Any structure, basin, chamber, or tank containing an artificial body of water for 39 swimming, diving or recreational bathing located in a residential area serving four or fewer living units 40 having a depth of 2 feet (610 mm) or more at any point as defined in Section 515.25, Florida Statutes, or 41 the body of water is a public pool as defined in Section 514.011, Florida Statutes. 42 43 ELECTRICAL INFORMATION AND EXPLANATIONS 44 45 3.01 ‚Äì Maintenance Items of Concerns 46 3.02 ‚Äì Is a Permit Required 47 3.03 ‚Äì Inspecting the System and Identifying the Parts 48 3.04 ‚Äì Applicable NEC Sections 49 3.05 ‚Äì Insulation 50 3.06 ‚Äì Lighting 51 3.07 ‚Äì Wiring Methods 52 3.08 ‚Äì Finding More Information 53Chapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 126 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 SECTION 3.01 - MAINTENANCE ITEMS OF CONCERN 2 3 As a service technician, it is imperative that you be the expert to protect your customer and their friends. 4 You have a responsibility to look at the overall system. Make 32 sure they are working, push the button and record it on the ticket. 33 34 Code requirements are based solely on safety. Manufacturer‚Äôs instructions must also be followed. Pumps 35 REQUIRE a minimum #12 equipment ground wire. Most pumps also require connection to the bonding 36 grid by a #8 solid copper conductor. The connection of these 2 conductors is a life safety requirement. 37 While the GFCI would seem to be sufficient for the safety of the overall system, this is the belt and 38 suspenders approach to assuring safety and proper operation. 39 40 GFCI Receptacle 41 42 The GFCI may be a receptacle, a circuit breaker, or even a GFCI protector. If 43 any portions of the protective systems are found to be missing or non- 44 operational, they should be noted and be sure to tell the owner. Never by- 45 pass or remove it. 46 47 If these items affect the life safety portion of the overall system, such as a 48 missing bond wire or GFCI that does not test properly, DO NOT TURN ON 49 THE SYSTEM and advise the owner that an electrician is required before 50 energizing. Always make sure the pump is grounded and bonded. 51 52 Be aware: the energy code may require a 2-speed motor. 53Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Reicon Publishing, LLC 127 1 Section 3.04 - Applicable NEC Sections 2 3 Be aware of and know how to find and reference each of these NEC sections: 4 5 680.6 Grounding 6 680.11 Equipment Rooms and Pits 7 680.12 Maintenance Disconnecting Means 8 680.21 Motors Requirements 9 10 1) General: The branch circuits for pool-associated motors shall be installed in rigid metal conduit, 11 intermediate metal conduit, rigid polyvinyl chloride conduit, reinforced thermosetting resin conduit, or 12 Type MC cable listed for the location. Other wiring methods and materials shall be permitted in specific 13 locations or applications as covered in this section. Any wiring method employed shall contain an 14 insulated copper equipment grounding conductor sized in accordance with 250.122 but not smaller than 15 12 AWG. 16 17 680.21 Motor Requirements 18 19 No matter the wiring method, the electrical code does not want to rely on the wiring method for the ground 20 fault return path. The required #12 insulated copper conductor, run with the circuit conductors, ensures a 21 low impedance path to clear any ground faults and, will hopefully be a lower resistance than any person 22 or other connection that may interfere with clearing the fault. Clearing the fault, means tripping the circuit 23 breaker or blowing the fuse. 24 25 The other code section referred to here, 250.122, is a table in the electrical code that provides the size of 26 the required equipment grounding conductor based on the size of the fuse or circuit breaker. 27 28 Section 3.04 - Applicable NEC Sections 29 30 680.21 Motor Requirements 31 32 A representation of wire sizes. There will be writing on the insulation indicating the size. Verify it! 33 34 35 36 On or Within Buildings: Where installed on or within buildings, electrical metallic tubing shall be 37 permitted. 38 Flexible Connections: Where necessary to employ flexible connections at or adjacent to the motor, 39 liquid tight flexible metal or liquid tight flexible nonmetallic conduit with approved fittings shall be 40 permitted. 41 42 It is often necessary to provide a flexible connection for the electric supply going to the motor. This 43 prevents the transmission of vibrations and helps ensure the ongoing integrity of the electrical connection. 44 Whatever acceptable wiring method is used, remember to be sure to use the proper fittings. Fittings for 45 these wiring methods are listed for specific types, such as metallic or non-metallic and type A or type B. 46Chapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 128 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 Section 3.05 ‚Äì Insulation 2 3 Metallic Flexible Conduit 4 5 The most common flexible wiring methods ‚Äì Metallic Flexible Conduit (Sealtite for outside, Greenfield for 6 inside), and Non-Metallic Flexible Conduit, probably the best option. 7 Each one requires fitting listed to be used with that wiring method. 8 9 10 11 Double Insulated Motor Bonding Requirement 12 13 Double insulated means the pump has an extra layer of protection and all the electrical parts are located 14 within a non-conductive housing. Where the bonding grid is connected to the equipment grounding 22 conductor of the motor circuit in accordance with the second sentence of 23 680.26(B)(6)(a), the branch-circuit wiring shall comply with 680.21(A). 24 25 A bonding wire does not have to be connected to the motor itself if the motor is 26 double insulated and the cord and plug to the motor is grounded properly. 27 28 New In The Current Code 29 30 (C) GFCI Protection. Outlets supplying pool pump motors connected to single-phase, 120 volt through 31 240-volt branch circuits, rated 15 or 20 amperes, whether by receptacle or by direct connection, shall be 32 provided with ground-fault circuit interrupter protection for personnel. 33 34 Section 3.06 ‚Äì Lighting 35 36 Be aware of and know how to find and reference each of these 37 sections: 38 39 680.22: Lighting, Receptacles, and Equipment 40 41 (B): Luminaires, Lighting Outlets, and Ceiling-Suspended (Paddle)Fans 42 43 680.23: Underwater Luminaires 44 45 Lighting in and around pools has always been popular. The hazard for lighting in 46 and around pools has always been a concern. In some cases, especially indoors, we may also have 47 ceiling lights and/or ceiling fans. We need to be sure if these are close to the pool, they are bonded and 48 protected by GFCI. There are locations that are prohibited from having these items. In any case, they 49 need to be listed for the use and location. 50Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Reicon Publishing, LLC 129 1 Wet-Niche Luminaire 2 3 A wet niche is currently the most common type of fixture and requires a bond 4 in the niche that attached to the bonding grid. As we move more and more to 5 LED, the next types of fixtures will reduce electrical consumption and the way 6 they are installed. 7 8 Understand the difference! Even if a wet niche is changed to an LED, the 9 bonding of the niche is required. 10 11 Dry-Niche Luminaire 12 13 Dry niche luminaires are dry and gasketed, they do not contact pool water. 14 15 No-Niche Luminaire 16 17 This style penetrates the entire shell with a waterproof housing. All service is done from the rear or 18 requires the pool to be drained for service! 19 20 Through-Wall Lighting Assembly 21 22 For through wall installations, a professionally installed pool transformer is still required. 23 24 Remember That Bonding Discussion? 25 26 NEC 680.23(5) Luminaire Bonding: The luminaire shall be bonded to, and secured to, the forming shell 27 by a positive locking device that ensures a low resistance contact and requires a tool to remove the 28 luminaire from the forming shell. Bonding shall not be required for luminaires that are listed for the 29 application and have not non‚Äìcurrent-carrying metal parts. 30 31 Note the wording here. We are counting on this connection to be sure the ring is bonded and grounded. 32 This may also serve as our water bond for the pool water. We need to ensure a low impedance 33 connection - no tie wraps! 34 35 Section 3.07 - Wiring Methods 36 37 NEC 680.22 Lighting, Receptacles, and Equipment 38 39 (A) Receptacles. 40 41 1. Required Receptacle, Location. Where a permanently installed pool is installed at a dwelling unit(s), 42 no fewer than one 125-volt, 15-or 20-ampere receptacle on a general-purpose branch circuit shall be 43 located not less than 1.83 m (6 ft.) from, and not more than 6.0 m (20 ft.) from, the inside wall of the pool. 44 This receptacle shall be located not more than 2.0 m (6 ft. 6 in.) above the floor, platform, or grade level 45 serving the pool. 46 47 2. Circulation and Sanitation System, Location. Receptacles that provide power for water-pump 48 motors or for other loads directly related to the circulation and sanitation system shall be located at least 49 3.0 m (10 ft.) from the inside walls of the pool, or not less than 1.83 m (6 ft.) from the inside walls of the 50 pool if they meet all of the following conditions: 51 Consist of single receptacles. 52 53 ‚Ä¢ Employ a locking configuration. 54 ‚Ä¢ Are of the grounding type. 55 ‚Ä¢ Have GFCI protection. 56 57 58 The general rule is to hard wire the pump. If the pump is connected by way of a receptacle and cord cap,Chapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 130 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 it must meet these requirements. 2 3 3. Other Receptacles, Location. Other receptacles shall be not less than 1.83 m (6 ft.) from the inside 4 walls of a pool. 5 6 Where a pool is installed at a dwelling unit, not fewer than one receptacle shall be located not less than 7 6ft. and not more than 20 ft. from the inside wall of the pool. This outlet must not be more than 6ft. 6 8 inches above the floor, platform or grade level serving the pool. 9 10 4. GFCI Protection. All 15- and 20-amp, single phase, 125-volt receptacles located within 6.0 m (20 ft.) of 11 the inside walls of the pool shall be protected by a ground-fault circuit interrupter. 12 13 5. Measurements. In determining the dimensions in this section addressing receptacle spacings, the 14 distance to be measured shall be the shortest path the supply cord of an appliance connected to the 15 receptacle would follow without piercing a floor, wall, ceiling, doorway with hinged or sliding door, window 16 opening, or another effective permanent barrier. 17 18 Section 3.08 - Finding More Information 19 20 This is the end of this presentation, but remember the learning does not end here: 21 22 As a licensed Swimming Pool Contractor, you have a direct impact on the quality of life for the public. 23 24 As a licensed Swimming Pool Contractor, you have an obligation to provide services in a professional 25 manner which protects the consumer from harm. 26 27 Please be sure to understand what you are working on and remember the codes are in place for safety. 28 Your understanding and attention will ensure a safe installation & maintenance. 29 30 Additional information regarding electrical systems and pools can be obtained by visiting: 31 32 Florida Swimming Pool Association 33 www.floridapoolpro.com 34 35 National Swimming Pool Foundation 36 www.NSPF.org 37 38 Association of Pool & Spa Professionals 39 www.apsp.org 40 41 United Pool & Spa Association 42 www.upsaonline.com 43Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 7 - Swimming Pool Electrical Safety Reicon Publishing, LLC 131 1 SECTION 4 - WRAP-UP AND FINAL COURSE COMPLETION 2 INSTRUCTIONS 3 4 Thank you for participating in our online Continuing Education course! 5 6 Please read each of the remaining slides carefully, as they provide important information regarding how 7 to: 8 9 1. EIGHTH EDITION (2023) 1 FBC-BUILDING 23 4 The Florida building codes that will be reviewed in this chapter are: 5 6 ‚Ä¢ Building 7 ‚Ä¢ Residential 8 ‚Ä¢ Existing Building 9 ‚Ä¢ Plumbing 10 ‚Ä¢ Mechanical 11 12 FLORIDA BUILDING CODE 13 14 The next section provides information on some of the key components of the FBC. 15 16 CHAPTER 1 SCOPE AND ADMINISTRATION 17 18 Chapter 1 is divided into two sections. Part I is Scope and Application; Part II is Administration and 19 Enforcement. 20 21 Part I ‚Äì Scope and Application 22 23 101.3 Intent 24 25 The purpose of this code is to establish the minimum requirements to provide a reasonable level of 26 safety, public health, and general welfare through structural strength, means of egress in facilities, 27 stability, sanitation, adequate light and ventilation, energy conservation, and safety to life and property 28 from fire and other hazards attributed to the built environment and to provide a reasonable level of safety 29 to fire fighters and emergency responders during emergency operations. 30 31 In general, the purpose of the building code is to provide public safety and safety of the emergency 32 officers in case of fire or any other catastrophic situations. The building code is the minimum standard of 33 construction. The safety of the building is to give any one trapped or rescuers 60 minutes to get out of the 34 building. 35 36 Reasonable degree of safety to public in the built environment: 37 ‚Ä¢ Code is for minimum requirements 38 39 Structurally sound buildings provide: 40 Life safety to public 41 42 Fire resistant construction provides: 43 ‚Ä¢ Life safety to public 44 ‚Ä¢ Life safety to firefighters 45 ‚Ä¢ Property protection for building and exposures 46 47 Egress provisions:Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Building Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 134 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 ‚Ä¢ Clearly defined exits routes that are accessible to evacuate the building quickly and safely in case 2 of a fire 3 4 These codes are available online for you to view at floridabuilding.org 5 6 This is especially important as this is the part that many contractors get in trouble with. A based on the nature of the hazards and 14 risks to building occupants generally associated with the intended purpose of the building or structure.
