The clear width of the stairway at and below the 17 handrail height, including treads and landings, shall be not less than 31 1/2 inches (787 mm) where a 18 handrail is installed on one side and 27 inches (698 mm) where handrails are provided on both sides. 19 20 Exception: The width of spiral stairways shall be in accordance with Section R311.7.10.1. 21 22 ‚Ä¢ Note: The criteria for handrail projections into the stairway width has been relocated to new 23 Section R311.7.8.5. 24 25 R311.7.2 Headroom 26 27 The headroom in stairways shall be not less than 6 feet 8 inches (2032 mm) measured vertically from the 28 sloped line adjoining the tread nosing or from the floor surface of the landing or platform on that portion of 29 the stairway. 30 31 Exceptions: Where the nosings of treads at the side of a flight extend under the edge of a floor opening 32 through which the stair passes, the floor opening shall be allowed to project horizontally into the required 33 headroom not more than 4 3/4 inches (121 mm). 34 35 The headroom for spiral stairways shall be in accordance wit1h Section R311.7.10.1. 36 37 R311.7.3 Vertical Rise 38 39 A flight of stairs shall not have a vertical rise larger than 151 inches (3835 mm) between floor levels or 40 landings. 41 42 ‚Ä¢ Note: The maximum vertical rise of a flight of stairs has been increased from 147 inches to 151 43 inches. 44 45 R311.7.4 Walkline 46 47 The walkline across winder treads shall be concentric to the curved direction of travel through the turn 48 and located 12 inches (305 mm) from the side where the winders are narrower. The 12-inch (305 mm) 49 dimension shall be measured from the widest point of the clear stair width at the walking surface of the 50 winder. If winders are adjacent within the flight, the point of the widest clear stair width of the adjacent 51 winders shall be used. 52 53 R311.7.5 Stair Treads and Risers 54 55 Stair treads and risers shall meet the requirements of this section. For the purposes of this section, 56 dimensions and dimensioned surfaces shall be exclusive of carpets, rugs, or runners. 57 58 R311.7.5.1 RisersChapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Residential Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 154 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 2 The riser height shall be not more than 7 3/4 inches (196 mm). The riser shall be measured vertically 3 between leading edges of the adjacent treads. The greatest riser height within any flight of stairs shall not 4 exceed the smallest by more than 3/8 inch (9.5 mm). Risers shall be vertical or sloped from the underside 5 of the nosing of the tread above at an angle not more than 30 degrees (0.51 rad) from the vertical. Open 6 risers are permitted provided that the openings located more than 30 inches (762 mm), as measured 7 vertically, to the floor or grade below do not permit the passage of a 4-inch-diameter (102 mm) sphere. 8 9 Exceptions: 10 11 The opening between adjacent treads is not limited on spiral stairways 12 The riser height of spiral stairways shall be in accordance with Section R311.7.10.1 13 14 R311.7.5.2 Treads 15 16 The tread depth shall be not less than 10 inches (254 mm). The tread depth shall be measured 17 horizontally between the vertical planes of the foremost projection of adjacent treads and at a right angle 18 to the tread‚Äôs leading edge. The greatest tread depth within any flight of stairs shall not exceed the 19 smallest by more than 3/8 inch (9.5 mm). 20 21 R311.7.5.2.1 Winder Treads 22 23 Winder treads shall have a tread depth of not less than 10 inches (254 mm) measured between the 24 vertical planes of the foremost projection of adjacent treads at the intersections with the walkline. Winder 25 treads shall have a tread depth of not less than 6 inches (152 mm) at any point within the clear width of 26 the stair. Within any flight of stairs, the largest winder tread depth at the walkline shall not exceed the 27 smallest winder tread by more than 3/8 inch (9.5 mm). Consistently shaped winders at the walkline shall 28 be allowed within the same flight of stairs as rectangular treads and do not have to be within 3/8 inch (9.5 29 mm) of the rectangular tread depth. 30 31 Exception: The tread depth at spiral stairways shall be in accordance with Section R311.7.10.1 32 33 R311.7.5.3 Nosings 34 35 Nosing‚Äôs at treads, landings and floors of stairways shall have a radius of curvature at the nosing not 36 greater than 9/16 inch (14 mm) or a bevel not exceeding 1/2 inch (12.7 mm). A nosing projection not less 37 than 3/4 inch (19 mm) and not more than 1 1/4 inches (32 mm) shall be provided on stairways. The 38 greatest nosing projection shall not exceed the smallest nosing projection by more than 3/8 inch (9.5 mm) 39 within a stairway. 40 41 Exception: A nosing projection is not required where the tread depth is not less than 11 inches (279 42 mm). 43 44 ‚Ä¢ Note: Section revised to clearly describe and emphasize consistent nosings and that nosing 45 projections are required at every walking surface throughout the stairway. 46 47 R311.7.5.4 Exterior Plastic Composite Stair Treads 48 49 Plastic composite exterior stair treads shall comply with the provisions of this section and Section R507.3. 50 51 R311.7.6 Landings for Stairways 52 53 There shall be a floor or landing at the top and bottom of each stairway. The width perpendicular to the 54 direction of travel shall be not less than the width of the flight served. Landings of shapes other than 55 square or rectangular shall be permitted provided that the depth at the walk line and the total area is not 56 less than that of a quarter circle with a radius equal to the required landing width. Where the stairway has 57 a straight run, the depth in the direction of travel shall be not less than 36 inches (914 mm). 58 59 Exceptions: A floor or landing is not required at the top of an interior flight of stairs, including stairs in an 60 enclosed garage, provided that a door does not swing over the stairs.Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Residential Reicon Publishing, LLC 155 1 2 See Section R311.3 for exterior doors where a step down is provided 3 4 R311.7.7 Stairway Walking Surface 5 6 The walking surface of treads and landings of stairways shall be sloped not steeper than one unit vertical 7 in 48 inches horizontal (2-percent slope). 8 9 R311.7.8 Handrails 10 11 Handrails shall be provided on not less than one side of each flight with four or more risers. 12 13 ‚Ä¢ Note: Section editorially revised to delete redundant language. 14 15 R311.7.8.1 Height 16 17 Handrail height measured vertically from the sloped plane adjoining the tread nosing, or finish surface of 18 ramp slope, shall be not less than 34 inches (864 mm) and not more than 38 inches (965 mm). 19 20 Exceptions: The use of a volute, turnout, or starting easing shall be allowed over the lowest tread. 21 22 Where handrail fittings or bandings are used to provide continuous transition between flights, transitions 23 at winder treads, the transition from handrail to guard, or used at the start of a flight, the handrail height at 24 the fittings or bandings shall be permitted to exceed 38 inches (956 mm). 25 26 R311.7.8.2 Continuity 27 28 Handrails for stairways shall be continuous for the full length of the flight, from a point directly above the 29 top riser of the flight to a point directly above the lowest riser of the flight. Handrail ends shall be returned 30 or shall terminate in newel posts or safety terminals. Handrails adjacent to a wall shall have a space of 31 not less than 11/2 inches (38 mm) between the wall and the handrails. 32 33 Exceptions: Handrails shall be permitted to be interrupted by a newel post at the turn. 34 The use of a volute, turnout, started easing or starting newel shall be allowed over the lowest tread. 35 36 R311.7.8.3 Grip-Size 37 38 Required handrails shall be of one of the following types or provide equivalent grasp-ability. 39 40 Type I. Handrails with a circular cross section shall have an outside diameter of not less than 11/4 inches 41 (32 mm) and not greater than 2 inches (51 mm). If the handrail is not circular, it shall have a perimeter 42 dimension of not less than 4 inches (102 mm) and not greater than 61/4 inches (160 mm) with a cross 43 section of dimension of not more than 21/4 inches (57 mm). Edges shall have a radius of not less than 44 0.01 inch (0.25 mm). 45 46 Type II. Handrails with a perimeter greater than 61/4 inches (160 mm) shall have a graspable finger 47 recess area on both sides of the profile. The finger recess shall begin within a distance of 3/4 inch (19 48 mm) measured vertically from the tallest portion of the profile and achieve a depth of not less than 5/16 49 inch (8 mm) within 7/8 inch (22 mm) below the widest portion of the profile. This required depth shall 50 continue for not less than 3/8 inch (10 mm) to a level that is not less than 13/4 inches (45 mm) below the 51 tallest portion of the profile. The width of the handrail above the recess shall be not less than 11/4 inches 52 (32 mm) and not more than 23/4 inches (70 mm). Edges shall have a radius of not less than 0.01 inch 53 (0.25 mm). 54 55 R311.7.8.5 Handrail projection. 56 57 Handrails shall not project more than 4 1/2 inches (114 mm) on either side of the stairway. 58 59 Exception: Where nosing of landings, floors or passing flights project into the stairway reducing the 60 required clearance at passing handrails, the handrail shall project not more than 61/2 inches (165 mm)Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Residential Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 156 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 into the stairway, provided the required stair width and required handrail clearance are not reduced. 2 3 ‚Ä¢ Note: Handrail projection limitations into the stairway width have been relocated from Section 4 R311.7.1 to new Section R311.7.8.5. A new exception has been added for instances where 5 handrails pass the projection of landing tread nosings and tread return nosings that project into 6 the stairway. 7 8 9 R322 Flood-Resistant Construction 10 11 R322.1 General 12 13 Buildings and structures constructed in whole or in part in flood hazard areas, including A or V Zones and 14 Coastal A Zones, as established in Table R301.2(1), and substantial improvement and repair of 15 substantial damage of buildings and structures in flood hazard areas, shall be designed and constructed 16 in accordance with the provisions contained in this section. Buildings and structures that are located in 17 more than one flood hazard area shall comply with the provisions associated with the most restrictive 18 flood hazard area. Buildings and structures located in whole or in part in identified floodways shall be 19 designed and constructed in accordance with ASCE 24. 20 21 ‚Ä¢ Note: Section editorially revised to clarify it applies to the repair of substantial damage not 22 restoration of substantial damage. 23 24 R322.1.1 Alternative Provisions 25 26 As an alternative to the requirements in Section R322, ASCE 24 is permitted subject to the limitations of 27 this code and the limitations therein. 28 29 R322.1.2 Structural Systems 30 31 Structural systems of buildings and structures shall be designed, connected, and anchored to resist 32 flotation, collapse, or permanent lateral movement due to structural loads and stresses from flooding 33 equal to the design flood elevation. 34 35 R322.1.3 Flood-Resistant Construction 36 37 Buildings and structures erected in areas prone to flooding shall be constructed by methods and practices 38 that minimize flood damage. 39 40 R322.1.4 Establishing the Design Flood Elevation 41 42 The design flood elevation shall be used to define flood hazard areas. At a minimum, the design flood 43 elevation shall be the higher of the following: 44 ‚Ä¢ The base flood elevation at the depth of peak elevation of flooding, including wave height, that 45 has a 1 percent (100-year flood) or greater chance of being equaled or exceeded in any given 46 year; or 47 The elevation of the design flood associated with the area designated on a flood hazard map adopted by 48 the community, or otherwise legally designated. 49 50 R322.1.4.1 Determination of Design Flood Elevations 51 52 If design flood elevations are not specified, the building official is authorized to require the applicant to 53 comply with either of the following: 54 55 Obtain and reasonably use data available from a federal, state, or other source; or 56 Determine the design flood elevation in accordance with accepted hydrologic and hydraulic engineering 57 practices used to define special flood hazard areas. Determinations shall be undertaken by a registered 58 design professional who shall document that the technical methods used reflect currently accepted 59 engineering practice. Studies, analyses, and computations shall be submitted in sufficient detail to allowFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Residential Reicon Publishing, LLC 157 1 thorough review and approval. 2 3 R322.1.4.2 Determination of Impacts 4 5 In riverine flood hazard areas where design flood elevations are specified but floodways have not been 6 designated, the applicant shall demonstrate that the effect of the proposed buildings and structures on 7 design flood elevations, including fill, when combined with other existing and anticipated flood hazard 8 area encroachments, will not increase the design flood elevation more than 1 foot (305 mm) at any point 9 within the jurisdiction. 10 11 R322.1.5 Lowest Floor 12 13 The lowest floor shall be the lowest floor of the lowest enclosed area, including basement, and excluding 14 any unfinished flood-resistant enclosure that is useable solely for vehicle parking, building access or 15 limited storage provided that such enclosure is not built so as to render the building or structure in 16 violation of this section. 17 18 R322.1.6 Protection of Mechanical, Plumbing, and Electrical Systems 19 20 Electrical systems, equipment, and components; heating, ventilating, air conditioning; plumbing 21 appliances and plumbing fixtures; duct systems; and other service equipment shall be located at or above 22 the elevation required in Section R322.2 or R322.3. If replaced as part of a substantial improvement, 23 electrical systems, equipment, and components; heating, ventilating, air conditioning and plumbing 24 appliances and plumbing fixtures; duct systems; and other service equipment shall meet the requirements 25 of this section. Systems, fixtures, and equipment and components shall not be mounted on or penetrate 26 through walls intended to break away under flood loads. 27 28 Exception: Locating electrical systems, equipment and components; heating, ventilating, air conditioning; 29 plumbing appliances and plumbing fixtures; duct systems; and other service equipment is permitted 30 below the elevation required in Section R322.2 or R322.3 provided that they are designed and installed to 31 prevent water from entering or accumulating within the components and to resist hydrostatic and 32 hydrodynamic loads and stresses, including the effects of buoyancy, during the occurrence of flooding to 33 the design flood elevation in accordance with ASCE 24. Equipment for pools, spas and water features 34 shall be permitted below the elevation required in Section R322.2 or R322.3 provided it is elevated to the 35 extent practical, is anchored to prevent flotation and resist flood forces, and is supplied by branch circuits 36 that have ground-fault circuit-interrupter protection. Electrical wiring systems are permitted to be located 37 below the required elevation provided that they conform to the provisions of the electrical part of this code 38 for wet locations. 39 40 ‚Ä¢ Note: New language added permitting equipment for pools, spas, and water features to be 41 located below the required base flood elevation provided the equipment is elevated to the extent 42 practical, is anchored to resist flotation and flood forces, and supported by branch circuits having 43 ground-fault circuit-interrupter protection. 44 45 R322.1.7 Protection of Water Supply and Sanitary Sewage Systems 46 47 New and replacement water supply systems shall be designed to minimize or eliminate infiltration of flood 48 waters into the systems in accordance with the plumbing provisions of this code. New and replacement 49 sanitary sewage systems shall be designed to minimize or eliminate infiltration of floodwaters into 50 systems and discharges from systems into floodwaters in accordance with the plumbing provisions of this 51 code and in accordance with Chapter 64E-6, Florida Administrative Code, Standards for Onsite Sewage 52 Treatment and Disposal Systems. 53 54 R322.1.8 Flood-Resistant Materials 55 56 Building materials and installation methods used for flooring and interior and exterior walls and wall 57 coverings below the elevation required in Section R322.2 or R322.3 shall be flood damage-resistant 58 materials that conform to the provisions of FEMA TB-2. 59 60 R322.1.9 Manufactured HomesChapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Residential Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 158 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 2 In addition to the applicable requirements of the state agency with jurisdiction over installation of 3 manufactured homes, installation of manufactured homes in flood hazard areas is subject to the 4 applicable provisions of the local floodplain management ordinance. 5 6 R322.1.10 As-Built Elevation Documentation 7 8 A registered design professional shall prepare, and seal documentation of the elevations specified in 9 Section R322.2 or R322.3. 10 11 R322.1.11 Structures Seaward of a Coastal Control Construction Line 12 13 In addition to the requirements of this section, structures located in flood hazard areas and seaward of the 14 coastal construction line shall be designed to resist the predicted forces of a 100-year storm event in 15 accordance with Section R3109 of the Florida Building Code, Building, and the more restrictive provisions 16 shall govern. 17 18 R322.2 Flood Hazard Areas (Including A Zones) 19 20 Areas that have been determined to be prone to flooding and that are not subject to high-velocity wave 21 action shall be designated as flood hazard areas. INTRODUCTION 7 8 Existing Building Definition 9 10 When is a building considered ‚Äúexisting‚Äù? The FBC-Existing Building volume provides guidance with the 11 following definition: 12 13 Chapter 2, Section 202 General Definitions 14 15 [A] Existing Building. A building erected prior to the date of adoption of the appropriate code, or one for 16 which a legal building permit has been issued. 17 18 There are some stipulations to the interpretation of this rule. Just because a building exists does not 19 make it legal. Just because a building exists does make it occupiable. Just because a building exists does 20 not mean it is being used as intended. 21 22 Rules governing occupancy can be found in Chapter 1, Sections 101.4.1, .2, and 101.7 outlined later in 23 this chapter. 24 25 CHAPTER 1 SCOPE AND ADMINISTRATION 26 27 101 General 28 29 101.1 Title 30 31 These regulations shall be known as the Florida Building Code, Existing Building, hereinafter referred to 32 as ‚Äúthis code.‚Äù In addition to the provisions of this chapter, the provisions of Chapter 1, Florida Building 33 Code, Building, shall govern the administration and enforcement of this code. 34 35 101.2 Scope 36 37 The provisions of this code shall apply to the construction, alteration, movement, enlargement, 38 replacement, repair, equipment, use and occupancy, location, maintenance, removal, and demolition of 39 every building or structure or any appurtenances connected or attached to such buildings or structures. 40 41 Exceptions: 42 43 Detached one- and two-family dwellings and multiple single-family dwellings (town houses) not more than 44 three stories above grade plane in height with a separate means of egress and their accessory structures 45 shall comply with the Florida Building Code, Residential. 46 Existing buildings undergoing repair, alterations or additions and change of occupancy shall be permitted 47 to comply with the Florida Building Code, Existing Building. 48Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Existing Building Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 162 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 101.2.1 Appendices 2 3 Provisions in the appendices shall not apply unless specifically adopted. [FBC-Building] 4 5 [A] 101.4 Applicability 6 7 This code shall apply to the repair, alteration, change of occupancy, addition, and relocation of existing 8 buildings, regardless of occupancy, subject to the criteria of Sections 101.4.1 and 101.4.2. 9 10 [A] 101.4.1 Buildings Not Previously Occupied 11 12 A building or portion of a building that has not been previously occupied or used for its intended purpose 13 in accordance with the laws in existence at the time of its completion shall be permitted to comply with the 14 provisions of the laws in existence at the time of its original permit unless such permit has expired. 15 Subsequent permits shall comply with the Florida Building Code, Building or Florida Building Code, 16 Residential, as applicable, for new construction. 17 18 Commentary: This section makes it clear that a building under construction when the code changes is 19 permitted to continue under construction based on the approved plans and permits. Changes to the new 20 code do not affect this construction as it is underway. A building that was not completed and has allowed 21 the permit to expire is different. 22 23 If the building was not completed, the permit expired, and the building never received a Certificate of 24 Occupancy, meaning it has not been used for the intended purpose, a new permit in compliance with the 25 code in effect at the time of application is required, This section further makes it clear that a building not 26 used for the intended purpose is also considered NOT previously occupied. This could be a building 27 constructed as a mercantile, Group M, and then used for assembly purposes, Group A, as an example. It 28 was not designed or constructed for that use. 29 30 [A] 101.4.2 Buildings Previously Occupied 31 32 The legal occupancy of any building existing on the date of adoption of this code shall be permitted to 33 continue without change, except as is specifically covered in this code, the Florida Fire Prevention Code, 34 or as is deemed necessary by the code official for the general safety and welfare of the occupants and 35 the public. 36 37 Commentary: Buildings that are in use and being used as intended are permitted to continue in that use. 38 This is the reason for the Existing Building volume. Just because the code has been updated, we do not 39 go through communities and require that each building be reconstructed. This is not only unfair; it is 40 unreasonable and costly. 41 42 [A] 101.5 Safeguards During Construction 43 44 Construction work covered in this code, including any related demolition, shall comply with the 45 requirements of Chapter 15. 46 47 [A] 101.6 Appendices 48 49 Reserved. 50 51 [A] 101.7 Correction of Violations of Other Codes 52 53 Repairs or alterations mandated by any property, housing, or fire safety maintenance code or mandated 54 by any licensing rule or ordinance adopted pursuant to law shall conform only to the requirements of that 55 code, rule, or ordinance and shall not be required to conform to this code unless the code requiring such 56 repair or alteration so provides. 57 58 59 Commentary: This and 101.4.2 that allows buildings to continue in use make several things clear. We do 60 not intend to apply the current edition of the code to existing buildings. A building being used as intendedFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Existing Building Reicon Publishing, LLC 163 1 can continue to operate in the capacity for which it was originally designed, this is true even if the current 2 edition of the code would require something different, such as the addition of fire sprinklers. If there are 3 items found in other codes, rules, or laws they are not required by this volume and are not to be applied 4 as if they were. Existing violations of life safety that may be present and discovered during alterations are 5 to be cited to the owner. 6 7 Often during an alteration or repair, existing violations of the codes are uncovered. Some of these may be 8 accidental or inadvertent. Some may have been caused by previous owners or tenants. The contractor 9 making the repairs or alterations may have been unaware of any issues. It may be outside of their 10 contract. Unless there is a life safety issue, those items found must be cited independently of any work 11 being performed. 12 13 101.8 Existing Mechanical Equipment 14 15 An agency or local government may not require that existing mechanical equipment located on or above 16 the surface of a roof be installed in compliance with the requirements of the Florida Building Code except 17 during reroofing when the equipment is being replaced or moved and is not in compliance with the 18 provisions of the Florida Building Code relating to roof-mounted mechanical units. 19 20 CHAPTER 5 6 CLASSIFICATION OF WORK 21 22 Alterations 23 24 An alteration can be defined as, any construction or renovation to an existing structure other than repair 25 or addition. Alterations are broken down into three categories, Levels 1, 2, and 3. 26 27 When doing an alteration, it is important to define the work area. 28 29 Work Area. A work area is that portion or portions of a building consisting of all reconfigured spaces as 30 indicated on the construction documents. A work area excludes other portions of the building where 31 incidental work entailed by the intended work must be performed and portions of the building where work 32 not initially intended by the owner is specifically required by this code. 33 34 503602.1 Alteration ‚Äì Level 1 Scope 35 36 Level 1 alterations include the Removal and replacement, or covering of existing materials, elements 37 equipment, and or fixtures using new materials, elements, equipment, or fixtures that serve the same 38 purpose 39 Does not include reconfiguration of space 40 41 Commentary: Typically, the component (delegate) engineer (such as engineering companies who are 42 building component design engineers) serves as the only engineer for this level alteration and do not 43 need to provide host reactions for others or require shop drawing approval. Since there is typically no 44 architect or engineer above the component engineer, the component engineer produces work either at 45 the master plan sheet (non-site specific) level or addresses a site-specific condition with the assistance of 46 the permitting contractor to develop a code compliant solution. 47 48 504603.1 Alteration ‚Äì Level 2 Scope 49 50 Level 2 alterations include the Reconfiguration of spaces, the addition or elimination of any door or 51 window, the reconfiguration or extension of any system, or the installation of additional system. 52 Reconfiguration of spaces (i.e., removal and addition of walls that could alter paths of egress 53 The addition or elimination of any door or window 54 Reconfiguration or extension of any system or installation of new equipment such as HVAC, electrical, 55 plumbing systems, etc. 56 Requirements for improvements or upgrade outside the scope of work exist in this category 57 Full compliance with the Code for new construction is not required 58 Applies where the work area is less than 50% of the aggregate area of the building 59Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Existing Building Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 164 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 Commentary: Typically, there is an architect and/or engineer of record (AOR/EOR) on level 2 alterations 2 that receives the component engineers‚Äô information for approval and incorporation in their design. 3 Component engineers cannot make the final decisions on design work and are subject to AOR/EOR 4 review and approval. The contractor typically facilitates coordination between trades, but at times the 5 component engineers work directly with the AOR/EOR for approval. Notes and disclaimers exist on the 6 component engineers‚Äô plans to ensure compliance and limited liability that they are serving as component 7 engineers. In cases where there is no AOR/EOR, the commentary from level 1 applies. 8 9 505 604.1 Alteration ‚Äì Level 3 Scope 10 11 Level 3 alterations apply Alteration Level 3 is just like level 2 alteration except where the work area 12 exceeds 50% of the aggregate building area of the building (total floors area). 13 Triggers in this classification are work that potentially affects the building‚Äôs fire protections systems, 14 vertical openings, means of egress, accessibility, and structural system. 15 16 Commentary: The notes from level 2 commentary apply with the exception that there would nearly 17 always be an AOR/EOR at this alteration level. The reconstruction or renewal of any part of an existing building for the purpose of its 31 maintenance or to correct damage. 32 33 It is important to note this does not state ‚Äúreplace.‚Äù A repair is intended to allow an existing portion of a 34 building or structure to perform as it was originally intended. 35 36 Chapter 6 4 of the Existing Building volume provides additional guidance. One of the important sections 37 for a repair is 601.2 401.2. 38 39 601.2 401.2 Conformance 40 41 The work shall not make the building any less compliant conforming than it was before the repair was 42 made undertaken. 43 44 Section 602 402 provides for the materials used for repairs. 45 46 Note: Chapter 6 Repairs has been relocated to Chapter 4. All systems need a 38 method of being shut off from the supply. 39 40 Every building was required to have a main water shut-off valve installed inside the home during initial 41 construction. So, for most emergencies or repairs, shutting off the proper inside valve is as important as 42 knowing how and where to shut off a buildings main water supply. There are also underground shut-off 43 valves installed at the property line; if shutting off this valve is necessary.Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Plumbing Reicon Publishing, LLC 169 1 Background 2 3 It is important to understand that different plumbing arrangements will dictate where the proper main 4 supply valve is located, such as the following: 5 6 Some buildings have the water meter located inside, while others are located outside, underground within 7 a ‚Äúpit‚Äù at or near the property line or right-of-way. Some homes also have submeters that are typically 8 inside even if the main meter is outside/underground. 9 Newer homes have fire sprinkler systems, while older ones generally do not. 10 Home construction also differs greatly as in basements, crawlspaces, and slab-on-grade. 11 Water shut-off valves may have round ‚Äúwheel‚Äù handles or lever handles. 12 13 Locating the Proper Valve 14 15 Basements: The shut-off valve is typically located near the front foundation wall. The main water may 16 have come through the concrete floor or through the wall. The valve is typically within 3-5 feet of where 17 the main water enters. In some cases, the main water may enter in a different area, like a mechanical 18 room, up through the floor, or near the water heater or furnace. 19 20 Crawlspace with no basement: The shut off valve will typically be located near the water heater or 21 under the kitchen sink, but anywhere is possible. As mentioned above, it may be inside the crawlspace; in 22 which case, you may want to consider a secondary valve located up in the living space (near the water 23 heater or under a sink). 24 25 Slab-on-grade construction: The shut-off valve will typically be located near the water heater or under 26 the kitchen sink, but anywhere is possible. 27 28 Choosing the Proper Valve to Operate 29 30 If a home has a fire sprinkler system, care is needed when selecting a shut-off valve. Unless it is a 31 sprinkler pipe that is leaking, you should only shut off the home‚Äôs plumbing supply and leave the sprinkler 32 piping charged/live. 33 34 If the water shutdown is for a broken sprinkler line or sprinkler head, then locate the first valve (closest to 35 the main water line entry point) and operate that valve as described below. 36 For all other general plumbing shut offs, emergency, and non-emergency: 37 38 For homes with fire sprinklers and an inside main water meter, locate and operate the second valve, it will 39 be above the main meter and past (downstream) of the fire sprinkler system ‚Äútee.‚Äù If you have a 40 submeter, its second valve will only isolate the irrigation and/or hose bibs. 41 For homes with fire sprinklers and an outside main water meter, locate and operate the second valve, it 42 will be past (downstream) of the fire sprinkler ‚Äútee.‚Äù 43 For homes without fire sprinklers and with an inside main water meter, either valve will shut off the supply 44 to the home but operating the second valve is a good practice and a safeguard if you are unsure of 45 whether the home has fire sprinklers or not. 46 For homes without fire sprinklers and with an outside main water meter, there will likely only be one shut- 47 off valve that will shut down the entire home.Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Plumbing Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 170 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 Backflow 2 3 A backflow preventer is a device that‚Äôs installed on a home‚Äôs water pipes that allows water to flow in one 4 direction but never in the opposite direction. Its sole job is to prevent drinking water from being 5 contaminated due to backflow. 6 7 How Backflow Happens 8 9 When water enters a home from the main water supply line, it should only ever flow in one direction - into 10 a home. 11 12 But sometimes, due to pressure changes in the pipes, that water can actually flow backwards and seep 13 back into the main water supply line. 14 15 For example, backflow can happen when there‚Äôs a break in the main water line or when a fire hydrant is 16 opened for use. Because pressure is lost during these events, water is no longer being pushed forward 17 into the home and will flow backwards into the city water lines. When this happens, backflow can 18 contaminate the public drinking supply with: 19 20 ‚Ä¢ Fertilizers/pesticides 21 ‚Ä¢ Human waste 22 ‚Ä¢ Chlorine from pools/spas 23 ‚Ä¢ Soap from sinks/dishwashers/showers 24 25 That‚Äôs where a backflow prevention system comes in handy. Let‚Äôs take a closer look at how a backflow 26 prevention system works. 27 28 Backflow Prevention System 29 30 Backflow prevention systems are devices installed onto a pipe that only allow water to flow in one 31 direction. Think of it as a one-way gate that allows water from the city‚Äôs public water supply to flow into a 32 home‚Äôs piping but stops water when it tries to flow backwards into the main water supply. 33 34 The parts you can‚Äôt see are described below. 35 36 Every habitable dwelling, and most commercial buildings have at least two plumbing systems, a potable 37 water supply and a drainage waste system (also called a drain, waste, and vent system). The potable 38 water supply system is used to deliver water to a building and to the fixtures within that building. 39 40 Once that water is used, and sent down the drain, the drainage and waste system are in charge of 41 disposing it; usually via the municipal sewage treatment system, or a septic system. 42 43 Supply lines: Water supply lines are installed for the purpose of supplying fresh water into the building. 44 In a bathroom, water supply line means connecting plumbing fixtures with the main water distribution line 45 of the building with pipes, so that water can reach them. 46 47 Federal law enacted in January 2014, requires pipe, pipe fittings, joints, valves, faucets, and fixture 48 fittings that are used to supply water for drinking or cooking to have not more than 25% lead content and 49 must comply with NSF 372. 50 51 Drains: Drainage is the method of removing surface or sub-surface water from a given area. Drainage 52 systems include all of the piping within a private or public property that conveys sewage, rainwater, and 53 other liquid waste to a point of disposal. The main objective of a drainage system is to collect and remove 54 waste matter systematically to maintain healthy conditions in a building. Drainage systems are designed 55 to dispose of wastewater as quickly as possible and should prevent gases from sewers and septic tanks 56 from entering residential areas. 57 58 The drain system within a home, works entirely by gravity, allowing wastewater to flow downhill through a 59 series of large diameter pipes. These drainpipes are connected to a vent pipe system that bring fresh air 60 to the drainpipes, preventing suction that would either stop or slow the free flow of wastewater. Vent pipesFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Plumbing Reicon Publishing, LLC 171 1 exit the building through one or more roof vents. Care must be taken to ensure that these roof vents do 2 not become clogged. Leaves and bird nests are two ways in which these roof vents can become clogged. 3 4 All wastewater flows to the main waste and vent stack. The main stack curves to become a sewer line 5 that exits the building near the foundation. In a municipal system, this is a sewer line. Where sewer 6 service is not available, the line runs to a septic system that is simply a mini-wastewater treatment facility. 7 8 ‚Ä¢ Traps: A plumbing drain trap is designed to retain a small amount of water each time the sink 9 drains, and this water standing in the bottom of the curved portion of the trap seals the drain and 10 keeps sewer gasses from escaping the drain and entering the home. The following types of traps 11 are prohibited: 12 o Traps that depend on moving parts to maintain the seal 13 o Bell traps 14 o Crown-vented traps 15 o Traps not integral with a fixture and that depend on interior partitions for the seal, except 16 those traps constructed of an approved material that is resistant to corrosion and degradation 17 o ‚ÄúS‚Äù traps 18 o Drum traps 19 ‚ñ™ Exception: Drum traps used as solids interceptors and drum traps serving 20 chemical waste systems shall not be prohibited. 21 o Each fixture trap shall have a liquid seal of not less than 2 inches (51 mm) and not more than 22 4 inches (102 mm), or deeper for special designs relating to accessible fixtures. 23 24 Sewer: Every building in which plumbing fixtures are installed and premises having drainage piping shall 25 be connected to a publicly owned or investor-owned sewage system, where available, or an approved 26 onsite sewage treatment and disposal system in accordance with F.A.C. 64E-6, Standards for Onsite 27 Sewage Treatment and Disposal Systems. 28 29 CHAPTER 2 DEFINITIONS 30 31 ACCESSIBLE. Water 30 conservation means using less water or recycling used water so that it can be used again. This type of 31 program is important because water conservation helps save energy, protect wild animals, and prevent 32 people from using so much water that it cannot be replaced with rain. Water conservation also helps 33 people and governments save money. 34 35 Water Conservation Requirements 36 37 604.4 Maximum Flow and Water Consumption 38 39 The maximum water consumption flow rates and quantities for all plumbing fixtures and fixture fittings 40 shall be in accordance with Table 604.4. 41 42 Exceptions: 43 44 Blowout design water closets having a water consumption not greater than 31/2 gallons (13 L) per 45 flushing cycle 46 Vegetable sprays 47 Clinical sinks having a water consumption not greater than 41/2 gallons (17 L) per flushing cycle 48 Service sinks 49 Emergency showers 50Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Plumbing Reicon Publishing, LLC 175 12 3 F.S. 553.963(3) Standards for Showerheads 4 5 (a) The initial minimum standards for showerheads manufactured on or after January 1, 1988, shall 6 require the limiting of maximum water discharge to 3.00 gallons per minute when tested 7 according to ANSI A112.18.1M-1979. 8 (b) Showerheads manufactured for use in safety spray installations shall be exempt. 9 10 Water Conservation during Alternations 11 12 When water conservation alterations are performed under Level 2, the codes that apply are found in FBC- 13 Existing Building, Chapter 8 Alterations Level 2, Sections 810 and 1010, as outlined below. 14 15 810.1 Minimum Fixtures 16 17 Where the occupant load of the story is increased by more than 20 percent, plumbing fixtures for the story 18 shall be provided in quantities specified in the Florida Building Code, Plumbing based on the increased 19 occupant load. 20 21 810.2 Separate Facilities 22 23 Separate facilities shall not be required for single-user facilities that are or will be provided in places of 24 public accommodation or commercial facilities (as defined in 28 C.F.R. 36.104). Lead was used throughout the United States for 23 water services that serviced homes and businesses up until the beginning of WWII. The fact that lead 24 was very flexible and extremely durable made it an ideal piping material. The use of lead for potable 25 water declined after WWII because of the dangers of lead poisoning. Lead pipes are no longer used and 26 are still being replaced to this day in some of our larger older cities across the U.S. 27 28 Federal law enacted in January 2014, requires pipes, pipe fittings, joints, valves, faucets, and fixture 29 fittings that are used to supply water for drinking or cooking have not more than 25% lead content. 30 31 Common Plumbing Materials 32 33 The following materials are commonly used in the plumbing trade. 34 35 Polyvinyl chloride (PVC): Of the different types of plastic pipe used for water supply, PVC has a wide 36 variety of plumbing uses, from drainage pipe to water mains. It is most commonly used for irrigation 37 piping, home and building supply piping, and pool and spa systems. PVC is often white, but it can also 38 come in other colors. You can often tell what it is used for by the colors and mark on the pipe. For 39 example, purple pipe with black lettering is used for reclaimed water. PVC also comes in a variety of 40 thicknesses called schedules. Schedule 40 is the most common for water distribution. 41 42 Chlorinated polyvinyl chloride (CPVC): It is often (but not always) a cream-colored or off-white plastic. 43 This type of pipe can stand temperatures up to about 180 degrees F or so (this depends on the 44 schedule), so it and can be used for hot and cold water inside of a house or mobile home. Since CPVC is 45 the same outside diameter as copper and PEX, the same push-fit fittings, such as SharkBite, will fit the 46 CPVC piping. 47 48 As with PVC, you should use primer and glue when making a CPVC joint. Also, make sure the glue you 49 are using says that is can be used on CPVC. This glue is usually a different color, such as orange, so you 50 know that it is used for CPVC. There are also "universal" pipe glues that are designed to work with many 51 different types of plastic pipe. 52 53 Cross-linked polyethylene (PEX): PEX is commonly used for hot and cold water in homes, and for 54 hydronic heating systems, such as radiant under-floor systems due to its resistance to hot and cold 55 temperatures. PEX is widely used for its competitive pricing, ease of use, and the long rolls of piping that 56 it can come in. PEX can be joined in many different ways including push-fit fittings and specialty PEX 57 tools used with crimp rings to secure the joints. 58 59 This type of pipe is mostly used for hot and cold-water distribution, as well as being regularly used inFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Plumbing Reicon Publishing, LLC 177 1 HVAC systems for refrigerant lines. Although once used in gas piping, this is no longer allowed in most 2 jurisdictions. 3 4 Copper: Copper piping works in both underground and above-ground applications, but copper can be 5 affected by some soils and should be sleeved if used underground. Due to the price of copper and longer 6 labor needed to install it, many builders are switching to alternative water distributions piping, such as 7 PEX. 8 9 Copper comes in different thicknesses that are labeled M, L, and K, with M being the thinnest. Copper 10 pipes can be connected in different ways, including compression fittings or with a torch so you can sweat 11 solder it. 12Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Plumbing Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 178 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 [This Page Left Blank Intentionally]Reicon Publishing, LLC 179 Chapter 8 1 KNOW YOUR BUILDING CODE! 2 EIGHTH EDITION (2023) 3 FBC-MECHANICAL 4 5 CHAPTER 2 DEFINITIONS 6 7 Section 201 General 8 9 201.1 Scope 10 11 Unless otherwise expressly stated, the following words and terms shall, for the purposes of this code, 12 have the meanings indicated in this chapter. 13 14 201.2 Interchangeability 15 16 Words used in the present tense include the future; words in the masculine gender include the feminine 17 and neuter; the singular number includes the plural and the plural, the singular. 18 19 201.3 Terms Defined in Other Codes 20 21 Where terms are not defined in this code and are defined in the Florida Building Code, Building; Florida 22 Fire Prevention Code; Florida Building Code, Fuel Gas, or the Florida Building Code, Plumbing such 23 terms shall have meanings ascribed to them as in those codes. 24 25 201.4 Terms not Defined 26 27 Where terms are not defined through the methods authorized by this section, such terms shall have 28 ordinarily accepted meanings, such as the context implies. 29 30 Section 202 General Definitions 31 32 BALANCED VENTILATION. See Section 506.5.2. 4 5 IDENTIFYING PARTS OF THE SYSTEM 6 7 HVAC 8 9 Heating, ventilation, and air conditioning (HVAC): refers to the different systems used for moving air 10 between indoor and outdoor areas, along with heating and cooling both residential and commercial 11 buildings. They are the systems that keep you warm and cozy in the winter and feeling cool and fresh in 12 the summer. They also filter and clean indoor air to keep you healthy and maintain humidity levels at 13 optimal comfort levels. 14 15 HVAC is a common term that people use, and most have a general idea of what it's referring to. On the 16 other hand, how does HVAC work? 17 18 Air Handlers 19 20 An air handler's main function is to circulate the air in the system, and the components surrounding it alter 21 the functionalities of heating, cooling, and air quality. 22 23 Although there are multiple ways and places you can install an air handler, the unit connects to the rest of 24 the home through a series of supply and return ventilation ducts that recirculate the air in each room. 25 26 Air handlers can be paired with an air conditioner, heat pump, and sometimes even a mini split system for 27 both heating and cooling functions. Additionally, air handlers can also be equipped with a heating coil to 28 provide supplementary heat. 29 30 Components of an Air Handler 31 32 Just like a furnace, air handlers contain a blower (fan), controls, and an evaporator coil that conditions 33 and recirculates the air in a home. The air handler works with the outdoor unit (a condenser or heat 34 pump) in a typical split system by using refrigerant lines that connect the two systems. 35 36 Depending on where you live, the parts that make up the system will change. The components listed 37 below are found in the typical heat-pump/air conditioning setups. 38 39 Blower motor: Moves air through the connected ductwork to circulate throughout the home. 40 ‚Ä¢ Single and multi-speed motors will run at set revolutions per minute (RPM) and are capable of 41 moving consistent amounts of air, measured in cubic feet per minute. 42 ‚Ä¢ Variable speed motors, sometimes known as an electronically commutated motor (ECM), monitor 43 changing conditions and adjust those outputs for maximized efficiency. 44 45 Plenum connectors: These are air-collection boxes that connect the supply and return ductwork 46 throughout the home to the air handler. 47 48 Air filter: Air filters protect the internal components by capturing particulates from the incoming air. 49 50 Condensers 51 52 A condenser is a device or unit used to condense a gaseous substance into a liquid state through cooling. 53 In so doing, the latent heat is released by the substance and transferred to the surrounding environment. 54 55 The four main parts of refrigerating and air conditioning systems are the compressor, condenser, 56 throttling or expansion valve, and the evaporator. The refrigerant leaving the compressor is in the 57 gaseous state, and at a high pressure and temperature. This refrigerant then enters the condenser where 58 it loses the heat to the coolant that can be air or water. After passing through the condenser, the 59 refrigerant is condensed but still remains at high pressure. It comes out in a partially liquid and gaseousFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Mechanical Reicon Publishing, LLC 181 1 state, then enters the throttling or expansion valve. 2 3 The three types of condensers are air cooled, water cooled, and evaporative. These are described below. 4 5 Air cooled condensers: Air cooled condensers are used in small units like household refrigerators, deep 6 freezers, water coolers, window air conditioners, split air conditioners, small-packaged air conditioners, 7 etc. These are used in plants where the cooling load is small and the total quantity of the refrigerant in the 8 refrigeration cycle is small. Air cooled condensers are also called coil condensers as they are usually 9 made of copper or aluminum coil. They occupy a comparatively larger space than water cooled 10 condensers. The two types of air-cooled condensers are natural convection and forced convection. 11 ‚Ä¢ In the natural convection type, the air flows over it in natural a way depending upon the 12 temperature of the condenser coil. 13 ‚Ä¢ In the forced air type, a fan operated by a motor blows air over the condenser coil. 14 15 Water cooled condensers: Water cooled condensers are used for large refrigerating plants, big, 16 packaged air conditioners, central air conditioning plants, etc. These are used in plants where cooling 17 loads are excessively high and a large quantity of refrigerant flows through the condenser. The three 18 types of water-cooled condensers are tube-in-tube or double pipe type, shell and coil type and shell and 19 tube type. In all these condensers the refrigerant flows through one side of the piping while the water 20 flows through the other side, cooling and condensing the refrigerant. 21 22 Evaporative condensers: Evaporative condensers are usually used in ice plants. They are a 23 combination of water cooled and air-cooled condensers. In these condensers, the hot refrigerant flows 24 through the coils. Water is sprayed over these coils. At the same time, the fan draws air from the bottom 25 side of the condenser and discharges it from the top side of the condenser. The spray water that comes 26 in contact with the condenser coil is evaporated, absorbs the heat from the condenser, and cools and 27 condenses the refrigerant. Evaporative condensers have the benefits of both water cooled and air-cooled 28 condensers, so they occupy less space. However, keeping the evaporative condensers clean and free of 29 scale is difficult and requires lots of maintenance. For this reason, they are not favored by HVAC 30 designers. 31 32 Ductwork 33 34 Ductwork channels conditioned air to vents, takes return air back into the system, and transports exhaust 35 air and waste heat to the exterior of a home. Ducts serve a triple purpose: They carry warmth in winter, 36 hold cool air in summer, and provide ventilation for better indoor air quality throughout the year. Because 37 they‚Äôre in use almost every day, the ducts need occasional maintenance to improve the longevity of the 38 heating and air conditioning equipment. 39 40 How it Works 41 42 The air conditioner (AC) processes two air sources: return air and supply air. Depending on the building‚Äôs 43 construction, there may also be exhaust air ducts in the home, especially if the home is a multi-story 44 building, but most single-family homes use only supply and return ducts. Supply air comes from outside 45 the building via the ventilation system. Return air goes through the filter and into ducts that mix it with 46 supply air in a space called the plenum. The air in the plenum cools slightly as it combines external air 47 and recirculated air, but it is not until the air moves over the evaporator coils that it loses most of its heat. 48 49 The ducts that carry air to vents are separate from the return air ducts that bring air to the plenum and 50 through the main blower. The filters that remove smoke, dust, and other particulate matter from return air 51 serve a vital purpose and help keep the AC system‚Äôs coils clean. Clean coils are efficient coils, because 52 the evaporator coils carry coolant ready for its freight of excess heat, the coils‚Äô fins must be free of soil 53 that could hamper thermal transfer. 54Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Mechanical Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 182 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 Types of Air Ducts 2 3 The two major types of air ducts are defined below. 4 5 Rigid ducts: Rigid ducts are typically more expensive to install, but they are durable and quiet if the 6 home has the space for them. Rigid materials are also easy to seal and clean. For forced-air systems, 7 rigid materials maintain air pressure better than flexible options, which is why many homes use them for 8 main trunk ductwork and use flexible materials for offshoots. The most common material for rigid ducting 9 is galvanized steel, but polyurethane panels with additional insulation are becoming increasingly popular 10 for new construction. Fiberglass panels are another possibility for rigid duct systems; they are well 11 insulated and lightweight. 12 13 Flexible ducts: Flexible ducting, or flex, allows the AC installation specialist to fit ducts into smaller 14 spaces and around existing architectural elements. Flex ducts are economical and compact, making them 15 a good choice for reaching vents near corners or close to the house‚Äôs eaves. Today, most flexible ducts 16 are made from metal coils wrapped with insulating materials and polyethylene plastic, but older ducts may 17 incorporate other plastics and metal films. When installing flexible ducts, ventilation technicians should 18 avoid bending or kinking the wire within the tubing. Over short distances of under 15 feet and with no 19 sharp bends, flexible ducts can approach the efficiency of rigid duct materials. 20 21 OTHER SYSTEMS 22 23 Exhaust Systems 24 25 An exhaust system is defined as, an assembly of connected ducts, plenums, fittings, registers, grilles, and 26 hoods through which air is conducted from the space or spaces and exhausted to the outdoor 27 atmosphere. It also serves to extract water and greasy vapor, smoke, fumes, toxic gases, obnoxious 28 odors, heat, and aerosols out of the building. An exhaust system is found in kitchens, bathrooms, or any 29 area that has a combustible energy source. 30 31 Section 501 General 32 33 501.1 Scope 34 35 This chapter shall govern the design, construction, and installation of mechanical exhaust systems, 36 including exhaust systems serving clothes dryers and cooking appliances; hazardous exhaust systems; 37 dust, stock, and refuse conveyor systems; sub slab soil exhaust systems; smoke control systems; energy 38 recovery ventilation systems, and other systems specified in Section 502. 39 40 Section 502 Exhaust Systems 41 42 502.1 General 43 44 An exhaust system shall be provided, maintained, and operated as specifically required by this section 45 and for all occupied areas where machines, vats, tanks, furnaces, forges, salamanders and other 46 appliances, equipment, and processes in such areas produce or throw off dust or particles sufficiently 47 light to float in the air, or which emit heat, odors, fumes, spray, gas, or smoke, in such quantities so as to 48 be irritating or injurious to health or safety. 49 50 502.1.1 Exhaust Location 51 52 The inlet to an exhaust system shall be located in the area of heaviest concentration of contaminants. 53 54 [F] 502.1.2 Fuel-Dispensing Areas 55 56 The bottom of an air inlet or exhaust opening in fuel-dispensing areas shall be located not more than 18 57 inches (457 mm) above the floor. 58 59 502.1.3 Equipment, Appliance, and Service RoomsFlorida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! The operating mechanism for such openings shall be provided with ready access so that 53 the openings are readily controllable by the building occupants. 54 55 [BG] 402.2 Ventilation Area Required 56 57 The minimum openable area to the outdoors shall be 4 percent of the floor area being ventilated. 58Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Mechanical Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 184 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 [BG] 402.3 Adjoining Spaces 2 3 Where rooms and spaces without openings to the outdoors are ventilated through an adjoining room, the 4 opening to the adjoining rooms shall be unobstructed and shall have an area not less than 8 percent of 5 the floor area of the interior room or space, but not less than 25 square feet (2.3 m2). The minimum 6 openable area to the outdoors shall be based on the total floor area being ventilated. 7 8 Exception: Exterior openings required for ventilation shall be permitted to open into a thermally isolated 9 sunroom addition or patio cover, provided that the openable area between the sunroom addition or patio 10 cover and the interior room has an area of not less than 8 percent of the floor area of the interior room or 11 space, but not less than 20 square feet (1.86 m2). It removes airborne grease, combustion products, fumes, smoke, heat, and steam from the air by 50 evacuation of the air and filtration. In commercial kitchens, exhaust hoods are often used in combination 51 with fire suppression devices so that fumes from a grease fire are properly vented and the fire is put out 52 quickly. Commercial vent hoods may also be combined with a fresh air fan that draws in exterior air, 53 circulating it with the cooking fumes that are then drawn out by the hood. 54 55 In most exhaust hoods, a filtration system removes grease (the grease trap) and other particles. Although 56 many vents hoods exhaust air to the outside, some recirculate the air to the kitchen. In a recirculating 57 system, filters may be used to remove odors in addition to the grease. 58 59 SPLIT AIR CONDITIONING SYSTEMS 60Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Mechanical Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 186 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 How does a Typical Split System Work? 2 3 A split air condition system is named as such because it has components that are located inside and 4 outside the home. It‚Äôs literally split into two pieces. It is as referred to as ‚Äúcentral air.‚Äù 5 6 Consisting of an outdoor unit with a condenser and compressor, and an indoor unit with a fan and an 7 evaporator coil, a split air conditioning system works by removing warm air from a home and cycling it 8 back as cooler air through a system of supply and return ducts. Powered by electricity, the compressor 9 pumps refrigerant through the system to collect heat and moisture inside the home. That heat and 10 moisture is removed from the home when the warm air collected inside is blown over the cooled indoor 11 coil that cools the air. The heat transferred into the coil during this process is pumped outside the home, 12 while the cool air is cycled back inside. 13 14 A split air conditioning system is particularly effective in warmer climates, as it effectively keeps homes 15 cool and reduces humidity levels. It also features a lower indoor noise level compared to free-standing air 16 conditioning units, since its compressor-bearing unit is outside of the home. Central air conditioning 17 systems, such as a split system, provide the added benefit of air filtration because, as a ‚Äúforced air‚Äù 18 system, air is drawn out of rooms through air returns and pulled through an air filter. This removes 19 airborne particles and results in cleaner, filtered air being redistributed to the home through air supply 20 ductwork. 21 22 Transfer the Heat 23 24 Heat is the amount of energy transferred due to temperature difference between two bodies. HVAC 25 equipment transfers thermal (heat) energy from one location to another. 26 27 Heat flows from areas of high temperature to areas of low temperature, according to the Second Law of 28 Thermodynamics. The HVAC system is responsible for reversing this natural flow, removing hot air from 29 buildings in the hot summers and placing it in buildings in the cold winters. 30 31 How does that happen? It‚Äôs all about the heat exchanger. A heat exchanger is a device that transfers 32 thermal energy from one medium to another. 33 34 How does a freon system work? 35 36 Refrigerant is a general term that refers to a chemical blend inside an electro-mechanical air conditioner 37 that can shift easily between liquid and gaseous states. The earliest refrigerants were toxic or 38 combustible, which made air conditioning impractical outside of industrial buildings. But the invention of 39 the first non-toxic refrigerant blend, known by the trademarked name Freon, allowed air conditioners to 40 enter home use and spread across the country. The current standard refrigerant blend for residential air 41 conditioners is R-410A, sometimes called Puron, which produces fewer greenhouse gas emissions than 42 the previous blend, R-22. 43 44 As refrigerant transmutes from liquid to gas and back, it absorbs and releases heat. This is how it is 45 capable of cooling down a home. The compressor changes liquid refrigerant into a high-pressure, high- 46 temperature gas. The refrigerant moves to the outside coil of the air conditioner and releases its heat 47 there through condensation, cooling down. By the time it reaches the indoor coil, it has cooled down to 48 the point that it causes evaporation when it meets the warmer indoor air. This siphons heat from the air, 49 cooling it down. The refrigerant, once again a liquid, returns to the compressor to restart the cycle. 50 51 The refrigerant will remain at the same level for the life of the air conditioner unless leaks occur. If the 52 refrigerant level (its charge) starts to drop, professionals need to repair the system as soon as possible. 53 Low refrigerant will not only lower the cooling ability of the AC, but it can also severely damage the 54 compressor. 55 56 Section 1401 General 57 58 1401.1 Scope 59 60 This chapter shall govern the design, construction, installation, alteration, and repair of systems,Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Mechanical Reicon Publishing, LLC 187 1 equipment, and appliances intended to utilize solar energy for space heating or cooling, domestic hot 2 water heating, swimming pool heating, or process heating. 3 4 1401.2 Potable Water Supply 5 6 Potable water supplies to solar systems shall be protected against contamination in accordance with the 7 Florida Building Code, Plumbing. 8 9 Exception: Where all solar system piping is a part of the potable water distribution system, in accordance 10 with the requirements of the Florida Building Code, Plumbing, and all components of the piping system 11 are listed for potable water use, cross-connection protection measures shall not be required. 12 13 1401.3 Heat Exchangers 14 15 Heat exchangers used in domestic water-heating systems shall be approved for the intended use. The 16 system shall have adequate protection to ensure that the potability of the water supply and distribution 17 system is properly safeguarded. 18 19 A heat exchanger is a device that transfers thermal energy from one medium to another. 20 21 1401.4 Solar Energy Equipment and Appliances 22 23 Solar energy equipment and appliances shall conform to the requirements of this chapter and shall be 24 installed in accordance with the manufacturer‚Äôs instructions. 25 26 Solar energy can be defined as, the transformation of heat energy that comes from the sun to electricity. 27 28 THE EVOLUTION OF MECHANICAL SYSTEMS 29 30 The Original - Ice on the Roof 31 32 In the 1830s, at the Florida hospital where he worked, Dr. John Gorrie built an ice-making machine that 33 used compression to make buckets of ice and then blew air over them. He patented the idea in 1851, 34 imagining his invention cooling buildings all over the world. But without any financial backing, his dream 35 melted away. 36 37 In 1881, after an assassin shot President James Garfield on July 2nd, naval engineers built a boxy 38 makeshift cooling unit to keep him cool and comfortable. The device was filled with water-soaked cloth 39 and a fan that blew hot air overhead and kept the cool air closer to the ground. The good news: This 40 device could lower room temperature by up to 20 degrees F. The bad news: It used a half-million pounds 41 of ice in two months ‚Ä¶ and President Garfield still died. 42 43 Systems Through Time 44 45 The Romans and Greeks used similar tactics to keep themselves cool. Though it was not until technology 46 accommodated advancements that the heating and air conditioning, we know today began taking shape. 47 1830s - Dr. John Gorrie of Florida wanted to provide a way to shorten recovery time for patients. 48 Believing that bad air was the root of illness, Gorrie used buckets of ice then blew air over them, resulting 49 in a cooling effect. 50 1861 - William Siemens learned how to super heat coils with electricity. He installed a fan to blow air 51 through it, inventing the first electric furnace. 52 1881 ‚Äì After President James Garfield was shot, to provide him relief, naval engineers soaked pieces of 53 cloth in cold water and ice, then spun a fan over them. 54 1902 - Willis Carrier of New York invented an apparatus of chilled coils to control the temperature and 55 humidity of a room. This machine was primarily utilized in businesses where high temperature and 56 humidity levels ruined products, like ink and paper.Chapter 8 - Know Your Building Code! : FBC-Mechanical Florida CILB 14-Hr CE Bundle 188 Reicon Publishing, LLC 1 ‚Ä¢ Carrier‚Äôs first air conditioning units were large, costly, and dangerous as they used significant 2 amounts of ammonia. These units are used only in sizeable buildings like hotels, textile mills, and 3 hospitals. 4 1914 - The first air-conditioned home was in Minneapolis, MN. Unfortunately, the HVAC unit was still 5 enormous. It towered at 7 feet and ran 20 feet along the room. 6 ‚Ä¢ For the next fifteen years, movie theaters were outfitted with heating and cooling systems. Being 7 able to provide a cool environment increased sales and attendance dramatically during the 8 summer, allowing businesses to boom all year round. 9 1922 - Willis Carrier made two huge upgrades to his heating and cooling unit system. First, he replaced 10 ammonia with dyeline, a safer coolant for heating and air conditioning. Then, he reduced the size of the 11 unit. This allowed for HVAC to be available in more places like office suites, shopping malls, and railroad 12 cars. 13 ‚Ä¢ Within the next few years, central buildings like the House of Representatives, White House, and 14 Executive Office Building were fitted with HVAC. 15 ‚Ä¢ Unfortunately, rapid advancements for heating and air conditioning stopped. With the start of 16 World War II, commerce shifted toward producing military equipment. However, once the war was 17 over, Americans found that the economy was stronger than ever. There was an enormous surge 18 of sales in home heating and air conditioning. 19 1942 - The U.S. built a power plant specifically to keep up with the growing load of electricity demanded 20 from heating and air conditioning. 21 1953 - Over one million HVAC units were sold. 22 1950s - Improvements to the heating and cooling systems continued to be made. By the late 1950s, 23 motors were made to work more quietly, refrigeration was more efficient, and the HVAC unit was 24 relatively compact. 25 1970s - The central air system took over single heating and cooling units. 26 2011 - Up to 100 million American homes had heating and air conditioning. Experts and engineers in the 27 HVAC industry continued to make improvements, making it safer, smarter, and easier to heat and cool 28 buildings. 29 30 IMPACT OF THE ENERGY CODE 31 32 Building energy codes represent a significant savings opportunity for U.S. home and business owners. 33 Model energy codes for residential and commercial buildings are projected to save (cumulative 2010- 34 2040): 35 36 $126 billion energy cost savings 37 841 MMT of avoided CO2 emissions 38 12.82 quads of primary energy 39 40 These savings equate to the annual emissions of: 41 42 177 million passenger vehicles 43 245 coal power plants 44 89 million homesReicon Publishing, LLC 189 Florida CILB 14-Hour CE Bundle Exam #2025-1 1.
